Approval 
Page II 
 
Approval 
This M. Sc. thesis is the original work of Frithjof Johannes Thiem, M. Sc. Industrial Engineering. The 
thesis has been prepared over six months during the spring semester from the 24th of January 2022 
to the 15th of July 2022 at the Department of Electrical Engineering at the Technical University of 
Denmark, DTU, in partial fulfilment for the degree Master of Science in Engineering, MSc Eng. 
This thesis aims to develop a systematic approach to the hazard analysis of collaborative robot 
systems, which is performed during the design phases of such systems. The conceptualised framework 
builds upon the literature published within the field and on expert interviews, which are conducted as 
part of this thesis.  
 
 
Frithjof Johannes Thiem – s202972

==PAGE:4==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Abstract 
Page III 
 
Abstract 
The present thesis aims at creating a systematic process for the hazard analysis of collaborative robot 
systems. Existing approaches are often built to rely on previous experience. This results in a challenge 
in the further adaptation of collaborative systems in industrial practice, especially for first-time 
automation customers. Assisting inexperienced designers of such systems will support and improve 
the creation of safe and comfortable working environments.  
To achieve this aim, the work is structured around the systems engineering problem solving cycle. 
Within this, a situational analysis lays the foundation through assessing the current landscape of 
hazard analysis both from a practical perspective through discussing the applicable standards as well 
as from a theoretical perspective through a literature review. Following, a systematic approach to the 
hazard analysis of collaborative robot systems is developed in an iterative way. The initial suggestion 
is based on an analysis of case-studies of robot applications. This proposal is iterated and improved 
on through a series of interviews. In total, twelve system design experts are interviewed about their 
experience and knowledge with regard to the hazard analysis of collaborative systems.  
The work results in the description of a set of hazards as well as suitable risk mitigation strategies. 
Potential sources of harm revolve around the robot system itself and the environment in which the 
system is placed. Since primarily mechanical hazards are considered, for example physical contact 
between human and robot system, they can be mitigated by reducing the interaction energy. This can 
be achieved by changing physical properties or by reducing the speed of the robot, for example with 
additional technical measures such as laser range scanners. Otherwise, access possibilities to hazard 
sources can be removed, or the robot system can be limited in its movement. This description of 
hazards and mitigation strategies and how they relate to typical use-cases of collaborative robot 
systems is the novelty of this work and lays the basis for the developed systematic process for the 
hazard analysis.  
In addition, aspects of the hazard analysis that receive limited attention from industrial practitioners 
but that are described by researchers have been discovered. Likewise, the experiences of the expert 
interviewees show where future research should strive to provide additional guidance and where 
future research priorities should lie.  
The thesis is developed in collaboration with Universal Robots (UR). This can limit the generalisability 
of the results, which should be taken into consideration when reading the work. The case studies 
consulted for the initial solution suggestion are published by UR as marketing material. Furthermore, 
all interview participants are in a business relationship with UR. Nonetheless, the chosen approach for 
this work provided new insights into how collaborative robot systems are being used today, what 
challenges are faced by industry experts, and how the hazard analysis for collaborative robot systems 
could be improved.

Impetus 
Page 1 
 
1 Impetus  
This work builds toward a more systematic and more broadly usable hazard analysis framework for 
the risk assessment in the design phase of collaborative robot applications. Even though it is vital in 
reaching a safe and certified system, the hazard analysis is currently ill-defined in theoretical research, 
and in industrial practice, it is mostly based on the expertise and experience of the person conducting 
the risk assessment. This limitation hinders users of collaborative robot systems. Solving this issue sets 
the motivation, which will be thoroughly investigated in this chapter. 
“We´re trying to lower the bar for what customers need to do to meet their cobot expectations.” 
Kim Povlsen, CEO, Universal Robots (UR) [1, p. 17] 
This quote by Kim Povlsen highlights the impetus for this master's thesis. However, before the impetus 
is introduced and discussed in more detail, one should clarify what the CEO refers to when saying the 
customer has certain cobot expectations. For this, one should first adopt a broad perspective with 
regard to the application possibilities of the often labelled cobots or collaborative robots. Why this 
term might not be the most suited will be elaborated on later.  
To broaden the perspective, the current trends of the fourth industrial revolution should be examined. 
As stated by Ivanov et al., a core principle behind industry 4.0 is that companies must be “dynamically 
and structurally adaptable to changes in the demand and supply environment through rapid 
rearrangement and reallocation of its components and capabilities” [2, p. 2]. This flexibility, which is 
further mentioned in definitions used in both industrial practice as well as in other research contexts 
[2], is also one of the key strengths that are advertised for collaborative robots [3]. Generally speaking, 
a collaborative robot is “a robot designed to collaborate with human workers” [4, p. 6]. The properties 
of collaborative robots, when compared to traditional industrial robots, as also quoted in theoretical 
research, can be summarised as follows [4]–[6]: 
• 
versatility and flexibility through relocation and task changes 
• 
easy to program through on-line programming and lead-through walk-through programming 
• 
small and lightweight, shared workspace  
• 
cheaper and profitable at single lot production 
According to Universal Robots, with these strengths, collaborative robots “have played a major role in 
enabling companies that might not have been able to afford industrial robots to start automating their 
processes” [4, p. 9]. This view is shared by Fast-Berglund, who concludes that “collaborative robots 
tend to be the first enabling technology within Industry 4.0 that SMEs1 chose to implement”[7, p. 7]. 
Typically, in practice one can observe collaborative robots being used in the following applications [5], 
[7]–[9]: 
 
1 Small and medium-sized enterprises.  
==PAGE:10==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Impetus 
Page 2 
 
• 
Handling (Kitting, Packing, Pick and Place, Machine Tending) 
• 
Joining and Dispensing (Welding, Gluing, Sealing, Painting)   
• 
Assembly (Pre-assembly, Final Assembly, Screwdriving, Part Insertion) 
• 
Quality Inspection  
• 
Finishing/Material Removal 
When considering these applications, a limitation that is also noted in ongoing research can be 
observed. As described by Adriaensen: “In today's industry, collaborative robots are still used relatively 
independently of their human colleagues” [10, p. 2], [11]. The point is extended by Villani, who, when 
describing typical applications, states that currently the robot is used as a tool for the operator with 
little own autonomy. The robot supports the human colleague with ´dull´ tasks such as moving 
materials, holding heavy objects or performing tests [5]. This limitation can be traced back to the topic 
of safety and risk assessment for collaborative robots. According to the machinery directive, it is 
mandated in the European Union that machinery being used is marked with a CE2-marking, which 
states that the machine has been constructed in a way so that it is inherently safe to use [12]. To reach 
a safe system, the machine manufacturer must perform a risk assessment during the design phase 
and some steps in the risk assessment process are currently mainly based on experts' experience and 
expertise [13]. As stated by Seanz et al., “the strict safety requirements pose a difficult challenge for 
system integrators and robotics applications designers” [14, p. 1]. Hereby, the impetus of the work is 
highlighted when again considering Kim Povlsen's quote, who strives towards lowering the bar for 
customers to meet their cobot expectations. Lowering the barriers posed through safety 
considerations is a key consideration, especially since safety aspects are at the core of collaborative 
robotics. This is noted by different researchers [5], [10], [14]–[16]. 
The perceived safety challenge is extended when considering the marketing focus on SMEs. They are 
typically less experienced in conducting safety assessments which leads to them lacking the 
competencies to fully achieve their automation potential on their own. Additionally, they have a more 
limited financial capital. This is part of the original argument as to why they would be a core target 
group for collaborative robotics. Therefore, they lack the financial resources to hire an external expert 
for conducting a risk assessment. This challenge in the economic attractiveness of collaborative robots 
for extended use in practical applications is also noted in research [10], [17]. In addition, the lack of 
experience is quoted as a challenge [13], [18]. 
However, when talking about the safety aspects of a collaborative robot, one must not disregard an 
ongoing debate about the interpretation of terms used in this context, as summarised by Vicentini 
[16], who highlights some issues that can come from using terms such as ´collaborative´ for marketing 
purposes. The “terrific commercial success of the collaborative semantic family including the 
deprecated term ´collaborative robot´” is the origin of misused terminology in the safety context, 
 
2 Conformité Européenne, French for „European Conformity“. 
==PAGE:11==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Impetus 
Page 3 
 
according to Vicentini [16, p. 6]. For this, it must be understood that a collaborative robot does not 
exist as such. Only the application in which the robot (or cobot) is being used can be collaborative. 
This is because any robot should be treated as an uncompleted machine as per the machine directive 
[12]. This confusion about the terminology has led on one end of the spectrum to labelling modes of 
operation like the Safety-rated Monitored Stop (SMS)3 [19] as collaborative, that are not truly 
collaborative in the sense that humans and robots execute a task together. In addition, non-physical 
modes of collaboration are mostly disregarded. On the other end of the spectrum, physical 
collaboration scenarios are often over-engineered to be able to label as many situations as possible 
as ´collaborative´ [16] to follow market trends and smart usages of robotics. 
For this work, the term collaborative robot system is defined based on the definition used in the 
revised standard for robot systems ISO 10218-2:2020 since this is the definition that will be relevant 
for industry practitioners and CE-certification going forward: A collaborative robot system is one 
where a situation can occur in which a portion of the robot's movements/robots sequence results in 
both the robot application as well as the operator(s) being in the same safeguarded space at the same 
time. Therefore, contact between the robot application and the human operator can occur both 
intended by the application as well as unintended. The robot application refers hereby to the 
combination of the robot system and the workpiece, where the robot system consists of the robot 
manipulator/robot arm, the end effectors/tools, any additional equipment like vision systems or 
sensors, and the task program [20]. This definition sets the centre of attention on physical interaction 
between human and robot system and therefore does not focus on other aspects of robot and human 
interacting to execute a useful task which might include sharing of material or information [16]. 
Concentrating on physical interaction is best suited for the scope of this project, which is to investigate 
the hazard analysis.  
Despite this debate and the ensuing confusion, Vicentini concludes that one should not spend more 
effort on inaccurate definitions but rather on improving the risk analysis process [16]. With this, the 
impetus for this work and the focus on hazard analysis are again highlighted. This perspective is also 
shared by Huck, who concludes his work by stating, “further research is needed to find an appropriate 
tool that optimally balances advanced capabilities and ease of use” [18]. Huck hereby refers to tools 
on two different levels of user support. On the one hand, he refers to assistance on an analytical level 
– i.e. “assistance with the core task of the risk assessment” [18]. On the other hand, he mentions the 
user guidance and usability level, where support for guiding the user through the procedure and 
through, for example, the documentation is provided [18].  
The need for support on an analytical level is especially relevant in the comparatively new field of 
collaborative robot systems. The machines should be able to be utilised in a flexible manner, which is 
currently not well supported by existing risk assessment processes. Furthermore, new hazard sources 
 
3 For a definition of SMS see Section 2. 
==PAGE:12==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Impetus 
Page 4 
 
through the extended (physical) collaboration and interaction between humans and robots make the 
hazard identification and risk estimation more challenging. In addition, new strategies for the 
mitigation of these hazards exist, where the application and especially the validation of these new 
strategies open further challenges to the user. Since these challenges are currently mostly solved by 
relying on individual expertise and experience, they show the need for support tools that can assist in 
performing a risk assessment.  
Balancing these needed advanced capabilities and ease of use of these tools is hereby of the utmost 
importance for the aforementioned SMEs. They face the need to operate their collaborative robot 
systems safely and to mark their system with a CE marking. To be able to do so, they must perform a 
risk assessment. However, since they are often first-time automation customers, they lack resources 
and knowledge. Furthermore, the risk assessment process is viewed as being too time-consuming. So, 
support is needed on an analytical level to combat the lack of experience and knowledge. However, 
the tools can also not be too complex since the time-related resources are also limited.  
In practical application, mostly tools offering support on a user guidance and usability level are used, 
so no guidance on an analytical level is offered. These challenges have also been observed by other 
researchers. However, novel tools, approaches, and methodologies proposed by current research do 
not address these challenges to the fullest for different reasons, according to Huck [18]:  
• 
Fragmentation: Only part of the risk assessment process is covered by proposed tools. 
• 
Complexity: Complex methodology that uses purpose-built system models. 
• 
Validity: Tools are not validated for real-world use. 
• 
Missing awareness from practitioners for ongoing research.  
This is where the impetus for this work is defined. One must work towards bridging the gap to meet 
requirements for the hazard analysis for collaborative systems where no specific methods are 
prescribed in the normative standards4. Hereby, one must consider the apparent trade-off between 
worker safety and catering to increasingly complex and versatile applications [10].  
 
 
 
4 In this work the words norm/standard/harmonised standard/international standard/normative standard will 
be used interchangeably to describe the same concept.  
==PAGE:13==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Impetus 
Page 5 
 
The impetus is summarised in Figure 1 below.  
 
Figure 1: The Impetus for this work summarised (own figure) 
To work towards this impetus, the thesis will be structured 
as follows. After the motivation behind this work is 
defined in Section 1 Impetus, the overall structure will 
follow the systems engineering problem-solving cycle [21] 
(see Figure 2). Therefore, first, the problem space will be 
further explored through the situational analysis in 
Section 3. For this work, this includes a literature review 
to assess the state of research, an assessment of relevant 
standards and norms, and finally, an analysis of typical 
collaborative robot system use-cases. After the situation 
is well understood, a specific goal for this work can be 
defined in Section 3 to improve the current situation. The 
methodology to reach said goal is described in Section 4.  
 
Next, a solution will be developed iteratively and evaluated by defining and testing different 
hypotheses. For example, hypotheses are defined and tested through a series of expert interviews. 
The process of defining a solution will be presented in Section 5. Finally, Section 6 will analyse the 
solution and form a critical reflection of the presented work. This section should also evaluate and 
decide whether the defined solution suits the previously defined goal and what new impulses for 
continued research emerge from this work. The work will close with a concluding chapter.

Situational Analysis 
Page 6 
 
2 Situational Analysis 
To start the project, one must spend some effort in the situational analysis with the purpose of 
“familiarization with the initial situation and the task, or generally clarifying it, understanding it, and 
creating a basis for the setting of concrete goals” [21, p. 45]. For this project, this means understanding 
the landscape of hazard analysis for collaborative robot systems from a practical standpoint and a 
theoretical standpoint. First, this understanding should be reached by describing currently applied 
norms and standards, as they convey the current state of the art in industrial practice. Second, it 
should be reached by conducting a literature search to assess the current state of research. In addition, 
the current situation must be understood by analysing typical use-cases and applications for 
collaborative robot systems. This is needed for a better understanding of the solution space. Finally, 
the situational analysis will conclude with a summary of the conducted work and with a discussion of 
limitations in the state of the art and the state of research.  
2.1 Review of Relevant Standards and Norms  
In the European Union, the machine directive applies to all machinery and hereby also to all 
collaborative robot systems. It specifies that a machine may only be placed on the market and may 
only be used if “it satisfies the relevant provisions of this Directive and does not endanger the health 
and safety of persons” [12, Ch. 4]. The primary objective of the directive is hereby to place safe 
machinery on the market and thus to prevent accidents. In addition, the directive specifies the need 
for the existence of technical documentation and instructions for the machine. With this, the 
obligation is placed on the machine manufacturer to be able to demonstrate that the machine 
conforms with the machine directive and that it is safe to use for the intended purpose. This can be 
achieved through carrying out the appropriate procedures, i.e. a risk assessment, for assessing 
conformity which are described in the machinery directive. They result in a declaration of conformity 
that specifies that the machine conforms to the directive, and they result in a CE-marking which is to 
be placed on a machine and which shows the machine's conformity [12]. 
Since the machinery directive generally applies to a variety of machines and systems, it offers the 
possibility to prove conformity by building a machine that conforms with harmonised standards [12]. 
These standards can provide more detailed and more specific information both on a procedural level 
but also on a technical level. For these purposes, international harmonised standards can be classified 
into three main categories [22]:  
• 
Type A standards, basic safety standards, procedural level  
• 
Type B standards, generic safety standards, generic technical level 
o B1: Particular safety aspects  
o B2: Particular safeguards  
• 
Type C standards, machine safety standards, machine-specific technical level 
==PAGE:15==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 7 
 
For the continued situational analysis for the scope of this work, two standards are to be considered 
specifically. On the one hand, ISO 12100 as the Type A standard for risk assessment and risk reduction 
builds the basis on a procedural level [22]. On the other hand, ISO 10218-2 as the Type C standard for 
industrial robots is the foundation on a machine-specific technical level [23]. Specific Type B standards 
will not be introduced.  
In addition to harmonised norms, one must consider technical reports. They are solely informative 
and they include information or data which differs from data typically published in an international 
standard [24]. For example, this could include data regarding the state of the art of a specific matter. 
In this project, ISO/TR 14121-2 should be considered as it provides additional data to ISO 12100 in the 
form of practical guidance and example methods for the risk assessment process [25]. 
Lastly, technical specifications must be taken into consideration. A technical specification “addresses 
work still under technical development, or where it is believed that there will be a future, but not 
immediate, possibility of agreement on an International Standard” [24, p. 1]. In this work, ISO/TS 
15066 must be considered as a technical specification that provides additional guidance for ISO 10218-
2 specific for collaborative robot systems [19]. It is already known that the contents of this technical 
specification will become part of the revised ISO 10218-2 [20], highlighting the need to consider this 
technical specification in the scope of this project.  
In the following, the aforementioned standards, technical reports, and technical specifications will be 
introduced shortly with regard to the purpose of this work. 
2.1.1 ISO 12100 
ISO 12100:2010 is the first standard that should be mentioned specifically since it lays the foundation 
for risk assessments and for other safety standards. This Type A standard is the basis for the entire set 
of Type B and Type C standards, and it aims at supporting designers of machinery in constricting safe 
to use and compliant machinery through providing an overall (risk assessment) framework. Basic 
terminology, methodology, and principles for risk assessment and risk reduction are introduced [22]. 
An overview of this standard is crucial in understanding the impetus of this work as well as for the 
continued situational analysis.  
In the risk assessment process, the first step is to define the machine use limits (e.g., use limits or 
space and time-related limits). System boundaries must be defined, and the intended use described 
to make the following process easier by framing it more clearly. Secondly, the hazards associated with 
the machine must be defined and the risk of these hazards must be estimated. These steps together 
make up the risk analysis. In the risk analysis, it is essential to follow a systematic process. This way, 
one can ensure to provide a valid effort to identify all relevant hazards and consider them in a 
consistent way. A complete hazard identification should consider both hazards that can be observed 
permanently as well as those that can appear unexpectedly. It is, however, limited to only those that 
can be reasonably foreseen. A successful hazard identification first identifies all operations performed 
==PAGE:16==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 8 
 
by the machine and all tasks performed by operators interacting with the machine so that this 
information can build the basis for identifying hazards. The risk estimation should, for all hazards 
identified in the previous steps, determine the different risk elements. Namely, the severity of harm 
and the probability of occurrence, which is made up of the exposure of persons, the occurrence of a 
hazardous event, and the possibility to avoid the harm, should be defined. 
The risk assessment is completed by evaluating the risk and by defining whether the risk has been 
adequately reduced. In the evaluation, the previous step must be completed thoroughly and 
consistently since the risk evaluation needs to consider how the different risks score compared to each 
other. The final step in the process is the risk reduction. This should systematically follow three steps. 
First, risks are reduced by inherently safe design measures. Second, by using safeguarding or 
complementary measures. And finally, by providing information of use [22]. If a risk reduction is 
needed, it can either be achieved by reducing the severity or the possibility of occurrence. After each 
step in the reduction sequence, the risk should again be evaluated to see if the reduction has been 
sufficient or if another measure is needed. Furthermore, the iterative nature of the risk assessment 
process prescribes that each introduced risk reduction measure must be assessed to evaluate whether 
new hazards have been introduced by the addition of the measure. The presented iterative risk 
reduction process shall be carried out separately for each hazard or hazardous situation and under 
each condition of use [22]. A schematic overview of the process is presented below in Figure 3. 
 
Figure 3: Risk Assessment Process (own figure based on [22]) 
==PAGE:17==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 9 
 
2.1.2 ISO/TR 14121 
To extend the introduction of ISO 12100:2010, one should also consider ISO/TR 14121-2:2012. This 
technical report provides more practical guidance on how to conduct a risk assessment that is 
compliant with ISO 12100 by describing various methods and tools for the individual steps that were 
introduced previously. Furthermore, exemplary risk reduction measures are described. One important 
message to be taken from this report is the notion that the exact choice of tool or method does not 
matter for the risk assessment process and the compliance with ISO 12100. The benefits of the risk 
assessment process are more in relation to being disciplined in following a systematic process rather 
than the precision of the results [25]. 
As an example, the described methods in ISO/TR 14121-2 for two steps of the risk assessment process 
should be introduced in the following. First, the hazard identification according to ISO/TR 14121-2 can 
follow two main approaches. On the one hand, a top-down approach would consider a list of potential 
harms and describe events and situations as well as hazards that could cause this harm. On the other 
hand, a bottom-up approach considers a list of hazards and defines for each of them how they could 
lead to a hazardous situation, event, and finally to harm to a human [25]. 
The only method specifically described in the technical report for the hazard identification is the 
application of forms. In this method, one would start by defining the limits of the machine. Secondly, 
the tasks or operations to be performed by the machine would be described. In the third step, one 
should examine the relevant hazards for every task. This can be done both with a top-down and a 
bottom-up approach. For this final step, tables in the appendix of ISO 12100 are referenced that 
provide an overview of origins of hazards, hazardous situations, or hazardous events [25]. However, 
this method is limited to the application of forms, checklists, and tables, which draws back to the 
impetus of this work that a more systematic method is needed for the hazard identification step in 
the risk assessment process, especially to support inexperienced users.  
Second, the risk reduction according to ISO/TR 14121-2 follows the same three-step structure as 
proposed in ISO 12100 (inherently safe design, safeguarding, information of use). The scope is slightly 
extended to include complementary risk reduction measures like an emergency stop. Some more 
concrete guidance for the other three steps is also provided. However, the information is limited in 
scope and applicability as it has been kept general so that it applies to different (types of) machinery 
[25]. Therefore, this structure might be unintuitive to an inexperienced person conducting a risk 
assessment. Even with the more practical information from ISO/TR 14121-2, a limitation can be 
observed in standardisation literature that might hinder inexperienced users.  
2.1.3 ISO 10218 
Extending the basis laid by ISO 12100 as a Type A standard, Type C standards provide more detailed 
information for specific types of machinery. In the case of industrial robotic applications, this standard 
is the ISO 10218-2:2011 which is the second part to ISO 10218-1:2011. The first part is regarding 
==PAGE:18==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 10 
 
industrial robotic arms and is aimed at robot manufacturers [26], while the second part considers 
applications and is aimed at system integrators and end-users – which is more applicable to the scope 
of this thesis. Specifically, ISO 10218-2 deals with safety requirements for the integration of industrial 
robots. This covers all life phases of the machine from design over operation until decommissioning, 
the necessary information for these steps as well as all components of the industrial robot work cell 
[23]. Basic hazards and hazardous situations for robot systems are described as well as requirements 
to reduce these risks. As mentioned, currently a draft updated version of this standard is being 
published for review. This new draft should be considered in part for this work. However, the main 
reference will remain the already published version of the ISO 10218-2:2011.  
An important distinction between the two versions of the ISO 10218-2 for this work to mention is that 
terms and definitions concerning collaborative robotics have changed. The original normative 
reference from 2011 used the terms collaborative robot and collaborative workspace, a collaborative 
robot being a “robot designed for direct interaction with a human within a defined collaborative 
workspace” [23, Ch. 3] and a collaborative workspace a “workspace within the safeguarded space 
where the robot and a human can perform tasks simultaneously during production operation” [23, Ch. 
3]. These terms are explicitly not used in the new draft since only the application can be collaborative, 
but not the robot or the workspace itself [20], [27]. Therefore, the terms collaborative application as 
“an application that contains one or more collaborative task(s)”[20, Ch. 3] and collaborative task as “a 
portion of the robot sequence where both the robot application and operators are within the same 
safeguarded space” [20, Ch. 3] are being used. This refers back to the discussion of terminology by 
Vicentini which was mentioned as part of the impetus [16].  
Nonetheless, the content of ISO 10218-2:2011 remains relevant. It provides more information and 
guidance for all steps of the previously introduced risk assessment process for robot systems. For 
example, checklists with typical hazards for robot systems extend the information provided in ISO 
12100 and lists for the task identification support the hazard identification process. In addition, robot-
specific information for example regarding the layout or safety requirements can support the user. 
This information is however focused on typical industrial robot applications and is therefore not fully 
applicable to collaborative applications. So, while they should be considered for users dealing with a 
collaborative application, some experience is necessary to assess the applicability of information.  
2.1.4 ISO/TS 15066  
To bridge the gap of ISO 10218-2:2011, meaning the lacking specific information for collaborative 
applications, a technical specification exists which provides the current state of the art regarding 
collaborative robotics. As mentioned, the information published in ISO/TS 15066:2016 will be included 
in the new version of ISO 10218-2:2020 [20], highlighting the need to introduce and describe the 
contents of this specification for the work on hand. Currently ISO/TS 15066 is still applicable. Since it 
is only a technical specification, however, the normative reference for certification remains ISO 10218-
==PAGE:19==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 11 
 
2:2012, with ISO/TS 15066 only supplementing this standard with additional guidance. Specifically, 
guidance is offered in the form of operational functions for collaborative applications [19]. 
The functions can be summarised as follows [19]: 
• 
Safety-rated monitored stop (SMS): the robot is safely stopped before an operator enters 
the workspace to perform a task.  
• 
Hand guiding (HG): motion commands are transmitted to the robot system by an operator 
through the use of a hand-operated device. 
• 
Speed and separation monitoring (SSM): the robot system and operator may move 
concurrently in the collaborative workspace. Risk reduction is achieved by maintaining at 
least the protective separation distance between operator and robot at all times and 
lowering the robot's speed. 
• 
Power and force limiting (PFL): physical contact between the robot system (including the 
workpiece) and an operator can occur either intentionally or unintentionally but it is 
ensured that these contact situations occur in regions for force and pressure so that they 
are kept below certain threshold values so that no injury occurs. 
Physical contact in PFL mode can be either described as a quasi-static contact, i.e., a 
´clamping´ contact where a human body part is trapped between a part of the robot 
system and another physical structure. Or as a transient contact, i.e. dynamic impact, 
where the human body part can move back freely after it has been hit by part of the robot 
system. Different threshold values exist to differentiate between the two situations.  
The limit values furthermore depend on the specific body region. 
The use of SMS as a collaborative mode of operation is criticised in literature, for example by Vicentini, 
who states that the inclusion of this mode has led to confusion about the term ´collaborative´ as the 
robot is simply a state of powered standstill without any motion being allowed [16]. Therefore, the 
new version of ISO 10218-2:2020 does not include this mode of operation but focuses rather on HG, 
SSM, and PFL. This work will therefore make the same distinction of only considering these three 
options for collaborative applications and disregarding SMS as a non-collaborative mode of operation.  
To be able to assess which operational mode is most suited for any given collaborative application, 
one must conduct a risk assessment. Orienting these safety functions to the outcome of a risk 
assessment is found to be lacking [15], again highlighting the impetus of this work and showing a 
limitation of the current standardisation framework. Another challenge is in the PFL collaborative 
mode. The application and validation of biomechanical limit values can be a challenge in itself, 
especially for inexperienced users of collaborative applications. For example, because specific 
measurement devices are needed. Furthermore, for some body regions, there are no limit values 
given, since “contact exposure to sensitive body regions, including the skull, forehead, larynx, eyes, 
ears or faces shall be prevented whenever reasonable practicable” [19, Ch. 5]. This limitation can make 
==PAGE:20==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 12 
 
the design of truly unguarded collaborative robot systems infeasible. In addition, the limits further 
complicate the design process since the machine manufacturer must validate and test that its machine 
conforms to these limits, for example by conducting a series of extensive measurements. All these 
requirements described in ISO/TS 15066 show the need for more guidance and a more systematic 
process for inexperienced users. 
2.2 Literature Review 
The second step in assessing the current situation with regard to the impetus of this work is to extend 
the assessment from a practical perspective through an assessment from a theoretical perspective 
with a literature review. To find relevant recent publications a search is conducted on Scopus. This 
database is chosen as it is the largest database for abstracts and citations of articles and conference 
proceedings. Hereby, wide coverage and the inclusion of high-quality publications should be ensured. 
The search was initially conducted on the 7th of February 2022 using the combination of keywords as 
indicated below in Figure 4. 
cobot 
OR 
collaborative robot 
OR 
collaborative-robot 
OR 
PFL robot 
OR 
PFL-robot 
OR 
power and force limited robot 
OR 
power force limited robot 
OR 
human robot interaction 
OR 
human robot collaboration 
AND 
risk 
OR 
hazard 
OR 
safety 
AND 
identification 
OR 
mitigation 
OR 
analysis 
OR 
assessment 
Figure 4: Keywords for the literature search (own figure) 
To limit the scope of the search, first only review articles and surveys are considered. This narrows the 
search drastically, from 739 papers to 15 papers (see Figure 5). After a peer discussion and through 
the experience of previous studies, three further review papers are included to be assessed. This 
leaves 18 papers for a first screening of the abstract and title. At this stage, three papers can be 
disregarded as they have a focus on the field of medical robotics and rehabilitation robotics, which 
does not suit the impetus of this work. Secondly, four further papers are excluded due to a different 
focus of the research. For example, papers focusing on teleoperation or exoskeletons are disregarded. 
This leaves eleven papers for a qualitative assessment. 
==PAGE:21==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 13 
 
 
Figure 5: Results of the literature search (own figure) 
The aim of the qualitative assessment of the included review papers is to identify the most relevant 
research streams for this work. These topics should be explored in more depth in the following, both 
by discussing the identified review papers and also by extending the analysis to other publications that 
were referenced in the review papers. The topics that should be explored in more detail are:  
• 
Hazard Analysis (i.e., Identification and Mitigation)  
• 
Task-Based Approach 
• 
Action Theory and Socio-Technical View 
Before introducing these focus areas, however, the assessed review papers should be summarised 
shortly to show the extent of the first stage of the literature review. Generally, the scientific works 
that were identified as relevant can be classified in two major categories. On the one hand, surveys 
talking about collaborative robot safety or about specific aspects related to the safety of collaborative 
systems were included. On the other hand, reviews concerning the current landscape of collaborative 
robotics in general were discovered. That these generic review articles were identified, even though 
a specific focus on the safety aspect was set through the search term, shows the relevance of safety 
issues in collaborative robot research and again highlights the impetus of this work.  
In the latter category, for example, Dobra and Dhir find safety to be one of the core factors of human-
robot collaboration (HRC) in their systematic literature review. Besides assessing current literature in 
six total clusters they provide possible directions for continued research [28]. Adding another view on 
the current landscape of collaborative robotics research, Arents et al. analyse current publications 
with regard to six predefined clusters. These clusters already show a specific focus on safety questions. 
==PAGE:22==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 14 
 
They can, for example, conclude that current research rarely addresses the safety standards 
introduced in Chapter 2.1 of this work with only under 50% of scientific literature in their assessment 
mentioning the standards. They also conclude that the safety aspect is crucial for HRC [29]. 
Rather than focusing on a specific issue of collaborative robotics, Hjorth and Chrysostomou take a 
different approach. They look at one specific use case (non-destructive disassembly) and provide a 
general review of the current HRC landscape for that purpose. Their findings with regard to HRC are 
still generally applicable, as they provide a review by discussing ISO Standards and enablers of HRC 
like collision control, workspace restrictions, and communication between human operator and robot. 
They conclude by also noting safety as a main challenge. In this challenge, their opinion is that the 
amount of task sharing is the main influence on defining the final implementation of a system. 
Furthermore, they highlight that only few articles from their study promote physical interaction [30].  
Continuing the discussion of use cases for collaborative robotics, Segura et al. analyse case studies 
that were identified in theoretical literature. From these 50 exemplary studies they conclude four 
structural components for collaborative systems (interaction levels, work roles, communication 
interfaces, and safety control modes). The safety control modes are hereby the previously introduced 
modes from ISO/TS 15066 (see Chapter 2.1.4) and they conclude that only SSM and PFL are suited for 
physical interaction and that SMS is used more as a non-physical interaction mode. Furthermore; they 
conclude that physical interaction requires specific physical features of the robot system as well as 
specific user interfaces [31]. 
Vicentini worked towards a similar scope in 2020, when he reviewed the current landscape by 
discussing three major properties of HRC, namely safety features, degree of physical Human-Robot 
Interaction (pHRI), and human activity. His view on safety features is not limited to those described in 
ISO/TS 15066. He also makes differentiations for physical interaction, which is according to him only 
applicable to low-power tasks both with accidental as well as voluntary contact. Hereby, accidental 
contacts are relevant for safety devices and safety concepts while voluntary concepts require 
extensive mechatronics design and control (compliance while preserving performance). The choice 
for the type of interaction depends on the application and task design [32]. A year prior Vicentini 
worked on a different, though not less relevant scope when he discussed terminology used in the 
context of collaborative robotics. An important finding from this work, which could also be observed 
in his later publication, was the focus on frequency of requested access to the shared worspace and 
(kinetic) energy, approximated through velocity/speed, as the main classification factors for the risk 
assessment. In his paper, he tries to classify safety functions (namely SSM and PFL) based on these 
parameters. In addition, he concludes that inaccurate definitions have led to some confusion. For 
example, the denoted fact that collaboration is not restricted to physical interaction. However, effort 
spent on an accurate definitions could be better spent improving risk analysis [16]. 
==PAGE:23==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 15 
 
In the research area of risk analysis for collaborative robot systems, Huck et al. provide a thorough 
literature review of novel tools and methods. Furthermore, they compare these novel methods to the 
needs of industry professionals by analysing a series of interviews. Their main finding is that further 
research is required to find a tool that balances advanced capabilities and ease of use. Practical users 
tend towards simpler tools and methods while novel approaches provide more support but need more 
effort to be deployed. According to them, the appropriate level of assistance must still be defined. 
Furthermore, different novel methods need to be integrated to be less fragmented and they need to 
be validated to be deployable in industrial settings [18]. The same need to learn more about industrial 
practitioners' views and needs is concluded by Dobra and Dhir as one possible direction for future 
research [28]. This gap in existing research is also part of the impetus for this work. 
Another perspective on the risk assessment process is provided by Adriaensen et al. who try to shift 
from a techno-centric perspective in HRC to a socio-technical perspective [10]. The opinion, that the 
current focus in HRC is techno-centric is shared by Segura [31]. To show that current analysis methods 
can benefit from systems thinking methods, Adriaensen et al. present and test three systemic safety 
analysis approaches after presenting a review of the current focus on containing kinetic energy and 
ensuring separation. Besides showing the feasibility of such methods and arguing for the need to apply 
a socio-technical view on the risk assessment to complement the energy barrier perspective for HRC 
safety analysis, they show some practical learnings from the focus on interactions between 
components and actors instead of individual failures. For example, they conclude that one must focus 
on mode management in safety assessments. Hereby, mode management does not even need to fail 
to cause malfunction, but suboptimal system understanding can also lead to a hazard. This shows the 
need for clear communication of mode effects to the operator as well as for operator training [10].  
This can also be observed in other research. For example, Gopinath and Johansen conclude from two 
case studies supported by a literature review that situational and mode awareness are key factors for 
the hazard analysis. Furthermore, they mention the need to consider hazards associated with limits in 
human cognition [33]. Continuing this trend, Grushko et al. focus a literature review on improving 
human awareness in HRC by focusing on the applicability of different communication means 
(graphical, acoustic, haptic feedback). They conclude that improving human awareness could be a risk 
mitigation strategy to inform the user about the technical status and dangers present in the system. 
However, the most intuitive way to convey information must still be found. Most likely a more reliable 
information transfer can be reached by combining different approaches [34]. 
The focus on cognition as a safety feature can also be observed in the last identified and included 
review paper. In this work Zacharaki et al. analyse safety features to minimize the risks of a HRC 
application. In total, they conclude a toolset of six existing overarching methods or themes for 
providing safety in HRC. Besides four safety features (perception, cognition, action, and hardware 
features) they include psychological factors and the risk assessment as relevant features. Within the 
==PAGE:24==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 16 
 
topic of risk assessments, one finding is the need for more systematic risk assessment methods and 
the need for validation of these systemised methods. This finding refers back to the impetus of the 
present work. However, while the last two factors were included, the work is mainly concerned with 
the technical factors to reduce risk and with different hazard mitigation strategies. One interesting 
finding to mention here is the apparent need argued by the authors for robots to be ´active explorers´ 
in the future, i.e. to have an active vision system [35]. 
As with the work of Zacharaki et al. when considering the review papers, a focus on different hazard 
mitigation strategies can be observed. The hazard identification itself is rarely considered in-depth, 
typically the presence of some form of mechanical hazard is always assumed to show the relevance of 
different mitigation strategies. This again highlights the research gap and the impetus for this work. 
This apparent focus on hazard mitigation will be further explored in the next subchapter regarding 
hazard analysis (i.e., both hazard identification as well as hazard mitigation) to build a better 
understanding of this subject for the purpose of this work. After that, a task-based risk assessment 
approach will be discussed before the socio-technical view will be explored in depth. The following 
subchapters will be based on results of the described first literature search, expanded and added on 
by other references.  
2.2.1 Hazard Analysis 
As mentioned, many reviews cover the hazard mitigation, though it might be under different names 
like safety features, safety skills, or safety actions. Some papers also utilize the previously introduced 
operational modes from ISO/TS 15066. One important discussion is the applicability of mitigation 
strategies and how they can be utilised best. How the landscape in current research looks like in 
relation to the hazard mitigation will be explored in Chapter 2.2.1.2. This chapter will discuss both 
contents from the already introduced review papers and add other relevant sources where applicable.  
Before that, since it is also the logical dependency and the chronological order in the risk assessment 
process, the hazard identification should be explored in chapter2.2.1.1. This will cover on the one hand 
methodologies that can be used for the identification of hazards and on the other hand some notes 
on the actual hazards that can be observed in collaborative robot systems. 
Since the focus on hazard identification is limited in the works that were assessed so far, it is decided 
to conduct a second literature search. For this search, the same terms are used as introduced in 
Chapter 2.2 (see Figure 4), however for the last term in the search only the word ´identification´ is 
included and the search is not limited to review papers. This search yielded 88 results on the 7th of 
April 2022. Based on the title and abstract these results are assessed with regard to their applicability 
to this work. This screening process leaves three journal articles to be assessed in more detail. Six 
conference proceedings are also deemed relevant, however, they will not be discussed in detail. 
Nonetheless, smaller findings, notes, or quotes taken from them may be used in the following 
subchapters.  
==PAGE:25==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 17 
 
2.2.1.1 
Hazard Identification  
The hazard identification is a step that is emphasised in its importance for example in ISO 12100, 
where it is labelled the “essential step in any risk assessment” [22, Ch. 5.4]. Nonetheless, the 
information that is provided for this step in the existing norms and standards is assessed to be non-
exhaustive [15]. In his review of the risk identification in the context of ISO/TS 15066 Chemweno et al. 
state that additional risk factors are often ignored [15]. A similar view is shared by Adriaensen et al. 
who conclude that ongoing research is currently often limited to safeguarding and collision avoidance 
and that hazards from the broader work system receive less attention [10]. Both works conclude with 
the need for more systematic methods as well as the need for more guidance in the hazard 
identification of collaborative root systems [10], [15].  
The need for an extended scope of the risk assessment is also discovered by Lee et al. who analysed 
systematic causes and root causes of historic robot-related accidents in Korea between 2010 and 
2020. One noteworthy finding of this work is that a disproportionately large share of accidents occurs 
in maintenance operations and that these operational phases are rarely specifically considered in the 
risk assessment. Furthermore, they note the need to extend the risk assessment to not only include 
physical hazards (mechanical, electrical, chemical, hygienic) as it is now but to extend the scope and 
also consider personal, job-related, as well as system-related hazard categories when assessing 
collaborative robot systems [36]. These two factors should be noted and considered for a novel 
method, as the extension of the risk assessment scope might help prevent accidents in the future.  
Broader considerations for hazards in collaborative systems also build the basis of the study published 
by Hoskins et al. who categorise hazardous contacts, potential energy exertion, operational failures, 
deviations from designing norms, human error, and others as potential hazards [37]. Similar trends 
can also be observed in other industries. One example is the shipping industry, where according to 
Krogh (Green Jacobsen A/S) both energy hazards and situational hazards must be observed, while the 
situational hazards can be grouped in organisation, people, working environment, and tools and 
equipment [38]. The hazard wheel as a tool for identifying hazards presented by Krogh is added in 
Appendix 1 for further information.  
These considerations could be part of a new approach for hazard identification for collaborative robot 
systems. Guichet et al. argue for new hazards that are present in collaborative robot systems but not 
in typical industrial robots. They extend the scope by looking at advanced robotics and including 
hazards that are, for example, concerning strengthened autonomy of robot systems. Regarding 
collaboration between human and robot they identify hazards related to mode confusion or 
communication errors, bad synchronisation between human and robot, and high contact forces [39]. 
The inclusion of the latter again highlights that the risk assessment is more challenging for 
collaborative systems. The mechanical hazards that were traditionally assessed for industrial robots 
must be analysed with more care since contact between human and robot is permittable.  
==PAGE:26==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 18 
 
Hereby, accidental contacts must always be considered hazardous (of differing severity) and require 
some care in the design of protective devices and measures [32]. Involuntary contacts could result 
from foreseeable misuses of the robot system or from hard to anticipate movement combinations 
between human and robot. Voluntary or planned contacts are part of the task planning of a 
collaborative system, and they require an extensive range of mechatronics design and control 
techniques for the machine to operate safely. Nonetheless, voluntary contacts could still lead to 
hazards in the case of a system malfunction/failure or in the case that the operator does not pay full 
attention, which will be explored in some detail later on. All systems with potential contact situations 
are restricted to low-power tasks [32]. 
Besides the notes on physical interaction between human and robot, Vicentini also has some more 
general noteworthy thoughts on the hazard identification for collaborative robot systems, since this 
step requires a substantial effort according to him. From his perspective, the analysis of the task of a 
collaborative system in the form of the analysis of the workflow and the layout of such a system builds 
the basis for a risk assessment, in which the frequency and the energy of a robot system are the most 
important factors to be assessed. With the frequency, he refers to the frequency of requested access 
by humans. And with the term energy, he refers to the kinetic energy of a robotic system or an 
equivalent mechanical figure. Relevant considerations for the kinetic energy could be the physical 
characteristics of the robot system, as well as the speed of the robot. The factors should be at the core 
of the hazard identification because they are defined for an application based on the intended use. 
Therefore, they can be considered before applying any risk reduction measures. Furthermore, the 
desired frequency and velocity are known at design time and could be changed if needed [32]. The 
applicability of this for the risk reduction or hazard mitigation will be reflected again in Chapter 2.2.1.2.  
Furthermore, in his work Vicentini has a short review of novel methods for the risk assessment for 
collaborative robot systems. He mentions and introduces the unofficial term computer-aided risk 
assessment, under which different simulation platforms, frameworks, and tools are shortly mentioned 
in his review work. Most importantly, approaches combining a model-based description of a robot 
system to analyse potential failures and hazards as well as approaches with task-oriented modelling 
are referenced [32]. 
A more extensive review of these novel methods is conducted by Huck et al., who also compare the 
reviewed novel methods to practical needs. Relevant for this work are the different tools that are 
categorised under System Analysis. Within this, Safety Engineering Methods (HAZOP-UML, STPA, FTA), 
Task-oriented Methods, Formal Methods (both see Chapter 2.2.2), Expert Systems, and finally 
Simulation-based models are summarised. As mentioned, when compared to industrial needs, these 
tools are often too fragmented, too complex, and lack validation [18]. An example that should be 
introduced is the computer-aided risk assessment tool presented by Awad et al. This tool allows a user 
to use components from a library to model a collaborative system. Certain rules, also defined in the 
==PAGE:27==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 19 
 
library, then show resulting hazards in the modelled system that would need to be estimated by the 
user and mitigated later, for which some guidance is provided in the tool as well [40]. While this 
approach has the potential to provide relevant guidance to users of collaborative systems, the 
extended libraries that would need to be built up and that would need to be validated could limit its 
applicability in practice.  
One of the Safety Engineering Methods that should be mentioned is HAZOP-UML, which is developed 
by Guichet [41] since the method has been mentioned by other researchers in their reviews [18], [32], 
[35] and because it has been used for several case-studies [41]–[43]. This method bases its analysis on 
a model of the system using unified modelling language (UML), in which use case diagrams, sequence 
diagrams, and state machines are used to partition and describe the system. These modelled elements 
of the UML diagram are used in the next step in a hazard operability (HAZOP) study. Potential 
operational hazards of the system are identified with the use of an adapted list of guidewords. This 
step can be extensive since the combination of all entities, attributes, and guidewords must be 
checked [41]. 
The use of HAZOP is for this reason critiqued by Chemweno et al. because of the combinatorial 
explosion problem [15]. Guiochet however developed a digital tool to manage this aspect of HAZOP-
UML and he has shown its usability through case studies [41]. Furthermore, the use of UML as a tool 
is assessed to be critical for tasks with a large sequence of steps since it would require large efforts in 
the modelling of the system [15]. This large effort is also recognised by the author who developed the 
method, especially since experts from different domains must come together for the modelling. He, 
however, argues that this effort is well spent since it allows better communication about the system, 
which would anyways be needed for the design of a system. It would furthermore allow the actual 
hazard analysis to be carried out by just one person, whereas typically a team of experts would need 
to come together for this assessment. This person would nonetheless require a high level of expertise 
as also recognised by Guiochet. A further limitation of this approach is the focus on operational 
hazards which are linked to the robot task [41]. 
This draws back to the previous finding that hazards from the broader work system receive less 
attention. Adriaensen et al. try to combat this by considering a socio-technical view for the risk 
assessment [10]. While this will be described in more detail in chapter2.2.3, some noteworthy findings 
for the hazard identification should be introduced. A finding, that is also shared by Gopinath and 
Johansen, is that situational and mode awareness should be considered for the hazard identification 
as a potential source [10], [33]. This is because accidents might occur if the human operator is not 
aware of the robot system state and if the machine moves in a way that is not expected by the human 
collaborator. Human cognition is limited and this limit must be considered a source of hazards [33]. 
The issue is also recognised by Grushko et al., who provide an overview of different methods of how 
human awareness can be improved. They see the lack of communication from the robot system to the 
==PAGE:28==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 20 
 
human operator as a potential source of hazards [34]. A concept that should be mentioned is the idea 
of long distance prediction vs. short distance control. Long distance prediction requires the 
recognition of the intentions of different agents. This could be understood as the identification and 
mitigation of foreseeable hazards. Short distance control deals with the actions and reactions between 
agents, which could be understood as the mitigation of unforeseeable hazards [44]. Since short 
distance control typically requires mutual reactive controls of the different agents, it also requires 
clear communication from the robot system to the human operator.  
Finally, Berx et al. add a valuable perspective to the identification of hazards. They also consider the 
perspective of socio-technical systems when identifying risk factors (RF) in collaborative robot systems 
that have been described in research papers between 2010 and 2020. Their work concludes with five 
risk classes that offer a system-wide perspective by extending the consideration for RF beyond 
technical factors. Specifically, they consider Human, Technology, Collaborative Workspace, Enterprise, 
and External as the groups of risk factors. These RF, according to Berx, also reflect the different socio-
technical dimensions. Hereby, the collaborative workspace is at the centre of the investigation since 
all hazards will manifest themselves in this space, no matter their origin. Furthermore, the other 
dimensions “feed” or support the collaborative workspace, meaning it could not exist without the 
other four dimensions. According to the authors, their identified RF could be the basis for an easy-to-
use tool for the risk assessment of collaborative robot systems. However, the importance of the RF 
would need to be analysed first and the result would need to be validated and field-tested [27]. 
Nonetheless, the overview of the RF should be presented in Table 1 to conclude this subchapter, since 
the classified RF are relevant to the scope of this work, and they should be considered for the next 
steps.  
 
 
==PAGE:29==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 21
