Regulatory  
included in regulations issued by the government (directives, 
laws) or any other recognised official body (ISO, EU-OSHA, ILO) 
Environmental conditions 
related to conditions outside of the collaborative workspace, 
e.g., weather conditions 
Table 1: Risk Factors according to Berx (own table after [27]) 
 
 
==PAGE:30==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 22 
 
2.2.1.2 
Hazard Mitigation  
Moving from the identification of hazards in collaborative robot systems to the mitigation of hazards 
(i.e., risk reduction as described in Chapter 2.1.1), the following chapter should introduce and describe 
ongoing research for this ensuing step in the risk assessment process. This will allow an extensive 
overview over the field of hazard analysis. First, a generic framework of different dependability means 
allows an initial categorisation. This broader view gives a valuable perspective to the focus area of 
hazard mitigation by setting it in context [39], [45]: 
• 
Fault prevention: Prevent occurrence or introduction of faults, part of general engineering for 
example as design rules.  
• 
Fault removal: Reduce the number and severity of faults through validation and verification 
during development and through maintenance during use.   
• 
Fault forecasting: Estimate current number, future incidents, and likely consequences of faults 
(risk analysis falls in this category).  
• 
Fault tolerance: Avoid system failures when faults occur.  
All these factors are relevant when talking about hazard mitigation. Since this work is occupied with 
the risk assessment during the design phase of a collaborative robot system, however, fault 
forecasting is at the core of this study. Estimating future incidents and possible faults are the main 
considerations when conducting a risk assessment. The removal and prevention of faults could then 
be classified as the ensuing steps in the risk assessment process where risks are reduced through good 
design practices, for example by following norms and standards, or through validation and verification. 
The latter could, in the context of collaborative robot systems, refer to force and pressure 
measurements as described in ISO/TS 15066. Finally, building fault-tolerant systems should also be a 
consideration in the overall risk assessment process. This is important so that the system can also 
withstand unforeseeable hazards. In industrial practice, this step could for example be achieved 
through defining and using the correct performance levels5 of safety components.  
Within the category of preventing faults, one could again consider human awareness which can play 
a major role in the hazard identification for collaborative robot systems. It could, however, also be 
used as a tool to mitigate hazards and reduce risk. As proposed by Grushko et al., a combination of 
graphical feedback, acoustic feedback, and haptic feedback could and should be used to achieve a 
more reliable transfer of information from robot to human. Any new type of notification system 
should be tested through user tests, to find a satisfactory approach that also complies with existing 
regulations and standards [34]. 
The risk that can come from reduced situational awareness is also mentioned by Dobra and Dhir. 
Generally, when talking about safety aspects of collaborative robot systems, human awareness is 
 
5 “level used to specify the ability of safety-related parts of control systems to perform a safety function under 
foreseeable conditions” [104, Ch. 3]. 
==PAGE:31==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 23 
 
however a rather new consideration and is only one of the many factors that must be considered. 
Some more general notes on current safety subjects in research are provided by Dobra and Dhir. In 
their review, they include mostly works that are occupied with studying physical contact situations 
through studying collision detection, collision avoidance, position following, or impact management. 
Furthermore, they find that speed plays a key role in collaborative robot systems since lower speeds 
are important in assuring safe operation while higher speeds are needed for effective and efficient 
operation [28]. 
Generally, to assure safe operations, different strategies exist besides the focus on robot speed. For 
example, safety features are identified by Zacharaki et al. [35]: 
• 
Robot perception for safe HRI: 
human in the loop – sharing controls between human and robot 
vision-based perception topologies – methods for detecting humans and human limbs 
• 
Cognition-enabled robot control in HRI:  
control and reaction methodologies with vision systems as well as system´s self-awareness 
• 
Action planning for safe navigation next to human: 
compliant control, compliant navigation, pre-, and post-collision control 
• 
Hardware safety features: 
user-friendly material selection, force-torque sensing, panic buttons, ISO & standards  
• 
Societal and psychological factors: 
social considerations, robot features 
Again, the inclusion of psychological factors highlights the importance of this consideration. The 
technical safety features identified by Zacharaki et al. align well with the findings by Dobra and Dhir. 
Robot perception could be used for collision detection and position following. Cognition-enabled 
control can assist in collision avoidance, the same holds for action planning. And finally, hardware 
safety features can improve impact management [28], [35]. This description is not exhaustive, and 
more overlaps exist between the safety features. It should simply show how the different works aim 
towards related concepts in the mitigation of hazards.  
Similarly, the European Committee for standardization has published a document describing different 
safety skills. The document aims to build a framework for testing procedures and validation of residual 
risks. For this chapter considering hazard mitigation the safety skills themselves are however more 
relevant. In the solely informative document (i.e. not normative) the following skills have been 
identified [46]: 
• 
Limit Physical Interaction Energy: Ensure that no injury occurs in an unintended physical 
contact situation, both transient as well as quasi-static, both intended use as well as 
foreseeable misuse.  
==PAGE:32==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 24 
 
• 
Maintain Safe Distance: Avoiding contact by stopping, changing the path, and potentially 
lowering the speed based on the distance between human and robot. Includes the ability to 
stop after a safety signal e.g., from an emergency stop button. 
• 
Dynamic Stability: Avoid falling over, especially relevant for mobile robots. 
• 
Limit Range of Movement: Limit the space reached by the robot or limit trajectory features 
such as speed, acceleration, curvature, and jerks. 
• 
Maintain Proper Alignment: Ensure compatibility between movements by the robot system 
and the joint human axis, especially relevant for exoskeletons and rehabilitation robots.  
• 
Limit Restraining Energy: Especially relevant for wearable robots where body parts are 
strapped to and moved by or in conjunction with a robot.  
As can be seen by the descriptions, the safety skills relevant for this work concerned with collaborative 
robot systems in the form of robotic arms are Limit Physical Interaction Energy, Maintain Safe 
Distance, and Limit Range of Movement. These mostly correspond to hardware safety features, but 
can also be categorised as perception and cognition-enabled control (Maintain Safe Distance) or as 
action planning (Limit Physical Interaction Energy) [35], [46]. They also connect to the previously 
introduced categories of collision detection, collision avoidance, position following, and impact 
management [28], [46]. 
Each of the described skills can apply to several robot categories, robotic devices, and applications. 
They can help to address the risks that arise when applying collaborative operational modes 
introduced by ISO/TS 15066 [19], [46] (see Chapter 2.1.4). The collaborative operational modes from 
ISO/TS 15066 themselves are also used in theoretical research. For example, Vicentini maps the safety 
strategies to different use-cases and to the combination of frequency and energy. As mentioned 
before, he sees these factors as the most important considerations in the hazard analysis. For 
applications with very high requirements for the frequency of access, he only sees hand-guiding as an 
applicable collaborative operational mode. For lower requirements for access, both speed and 
separation monitoring, as well as power and force limiting, could be used. SSM would be, according 
to him, suited for higher energy applications, while PFL is the most suitable operational mode for lower 
energy tasks. In applications with very high energy, for example with very high robot speeds or with 
hazardous mechanical properties like sharp edges, neither collaborative mode can be considered since 
it would be unpractical. Finally, applications with very low requirements for access are not applicable 
for collaboration since the infrequent access would make the design inefficient [32]. For more context 
of his qualitative mapping of Energy vs Frequency, as well as the classification of the different 
collaboration modes and different application types to these factors, a figure from [32] is added to 
this work in Appendix 2. 
 
==PAGE:33==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 25 
 
A further study in which the operational modes are used was published by Chemweno et al., who 
describe how safety assurances and design safeguards such as the different operational modes can be 
oriented with outcomes of hazard analysis. Besides the operational modes, the authors include 
considerations for the design of the collaborative workspace and the design of collaborative robot 
operation. Summarised, these are the considerations they mention concerning hazard mitigation [15]: 
• 
Design of collaborative workspaces: Safety-oriented position of the robot system in the 
collaborative workspace and use of safety-rated soft axes complemented by emergency hard 
stops.  
• 
Design of collaborative robot operation: Design of safeguards, location of stopping functions, 
and provision of pendant control with enabling devices.  
• 
Encompassing collaborative robot operations: (SMS), HG, SSM, PFL with special consideration 
for workpiece and end-effector related hazards. 
A structured framework to orient these safeguards is however still missing according to the authors 
and, moreover, guidance is needed in relation to performing the risk assessment for collaborative 
robot systems. For example, a future framework may include more structured alignments between 
design safeguards and hazards that are likely to arise in collaborative systems [15]. 
All safety actions and safety features that have been described so far fall into the three-step process 
of risk reduction described in ISO 12100 (see Chapter 2.1.1) either in the hazard elimination/risk 
reduction by design, safeguarding, or complementary measures. Compare et al. in [47] extend this 
view by also considering information for use. In their study, the authors cluster 18 identified safety 
actions under these categories and map, which of the risk factors (Severity, Frequency, Probability, 
Possibility of avoidance – see Chapter 2.1.1) can be reduced through the use of these safety actions. 
The identified safety actions are herby based on ISO/TR 14121-2 [47]. Since the described framework 
is rather extensive but still valuable to this work, it is presented in Appendix 3.  
To summarize this chapter for this work concerning collaborative robot systems, the considerations 
for hazard mitigation based on all previously introduced frameworks will be defined in a systematic 
overview. This does not strive toward 100% coverage of all possible mitigation strategies, since that 
would not be feasible. It rather strives toward summarising different strategies and frameworks used 
for hazard mitigation in a way that they are usable for this work. The overview based on [15], [22], 
[28], [34], [35], [46] is defined as follows: 
 
 
==PAGE:34==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 26 
 
1. Hazard Elimination by Design  
a. Use of non-collaborative operational modes depending on the Energy/Frequency 
combination of the system  
b. Fencing/physical separation → no collaboration between robot and human 
2. Risk reduction by design  
a. Use of collaborative modes suited to the combination of energy and frequency  
i. Speed and Separation Monitoring (Maintain safe distance/position following 
and collision avoidance)  
1. Robot perception (vision-based or inertial sensor-based) 
2. Cognition-enabled robot control/Human-aware navigation  
3. Calculating safe separation distances and safe limiting speeds  
ii. Power and Force Limiting (Limit physical interaction energy/collision 
detection and impact management)  
1. Force and Torque limits and robot systems capable of sensing these  
2. Reduced speeds 
3. Passive safeguards, increasing contact surface to limit the severity  
iii. Hand guiding (Limit physical interaction energy/ collision detection and 
impact management) 
1. Mode management and mode awareness  
2. Enabling devices to activate HG mode 
3. Force and Torque limits and robot systems capable of sensing these 
b. Design of collaborative workspaces/Layout  
i. Limit range of movement, e.g., through soft axes/ Limiting devices 
ii. Prevent access to hazardous zones e.g., through fixed guards 
iii. Position of the robotic systems in the collaborative workspaces 
3. Safeguarding  
a. Safety components  
i. Laser Range Scanner  
ii. Light Fence 
b. Design of collaborative robot operation 
i. Action planning / Task planning  
ii. Use of enabling devices (HG)  
c. Hardware safety features (User-friendly material selection, energy absorption 
padding)  
4. Complementary measures  
a. Use of emergency stops (Maintain safe distance) 
b. Personal protective equipment (PPE)  
5. Information for use 
a. Combination of graphical feedback, acoustic feedback, and haptic feedback 
b. Training for operators  
c. Mode awareness  
 
==PAGE:35==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 27 
 
2.2.2 Task-Based Approach  
Motivated by Vicentini, who states in his previously cited work that the risk assessment and reduction 
are “based on an analysis of key elements in a human-robot task” [32, p. 6] (i.e., the application 
workflow, the layout, and assignment of roles to robots/humans) [32], a task-based view on the risk 
assessment process should be explored further. The notation of task-based hereby refers to a risk 
assessment that is primarily based on an analysis of the tasks carried out by robot and/or human in a 
collaborative robot system. With this, the main aspects of collaborative robot systems according to 
Vicentini in [32] are set in order by first looking at task planning, before considering safety aspects, 
and finally mechanism to reach safe systems (i.e., mitigation strategies). The naming convention used 
in this work, specifically to speak of a task-based view, is adapted from the work of Marvel et al., which 
is a work often cited in other ongoing research occupied with Characterizing Task-Based Human–
Robot Collaboration Safety in Manufacturing [48]. 
In their work Marvel et al. present a new, task-based methodology for describing the safety aspect of 
collaborative robot systems. In this, risks are evaluated based on activities instead of the environment. 
This is achieved in a methodology that follows three main steps. First, the task at hand is decomposed 
into smaller subtasks and subgoals. This step could also be used to define the responsibilities of human 
and robot in a given system by assigning the decomposed tasks to either actor. Second, based on this 
decomposition, risks are assessed. For this purpose, the authors present an ontology in which different 
subjects that can be part of the decomposed task system, as well as capabilities and common hazards 
of these subjects, are described. Noteworthy is that these common hazards are split into the 
categories of Tooling, Physical Interaction, and Ergonomics. The third and final step in the presented 
methodology is the abatement of risks, in which, according to the authors, ideally the likelihood and 
the severity of the risk should be reduced. For this step, they do not provide systematic guidance, but 
rather just state that risk reduction could be achieved with different measures, like choosing 
alternative collaboration types, changing tools, changing task processes, or adding safeguards. 
Furthermore, they state that risk could be reduced by changing settings like applied force or robot 
speed, adding personal protective equipment, or by changing the physical characteristics of the 
system, for example by adding padding or rounding sharp edges. The task of choosing between these 
different possibilities for reducing risks is left to the system designer without specific guidance. The 
measures are also noted in the systematic overview presented in Chapter 2.2.1.2 of this work. The 
authors conclude their work with the presentation of a case study and with some final remarks on 
their methodology. Specifically, they open the possibility of building a database of similar tasks and 
sub-tasks that could be used to achieve partial or full automation of the risk assessment process [48]. 
Similarly, Gopinath and Johansen independently developed a related approach. Even though they do 
not base their work on each other, the authors of this work also developed a three-step process that 
starts with the decomposition of tasks of a collaborative robot system and the critical analysis for the 
hazards of these decomposed subtasks. For this step, compared to the previously introduced work, 
==PAGE:36==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 28 
 
limited guidance on the identification of hazards is given. Gopinath and Johansen only suggest 
focusing the identification on interactions between participants in the collaborative task. In the third 
and final steps of the methodology presented by the authors, the causal factors of hazards and effects 
of hazards are documented. This information could build the basis for risk mitigation, where again no 
more systematic guidance is offered. The authors state that the presented methodology could be used 
early on in the design stage to gain knowledge about the system and that “higher fidelity analysis 
methods to assess risks” [49, p. 5] could possibly be applied later on in the design process [49]. 
Finally, also utilising a task-based perspective in developing a semi-automated procedure for risk 
assessment that conforms with ISO 12100, in a further work by Vicentini et al. the authors base their 
approach on the analysis of how tasks are carried out. Their method SAFER-HRC (Safety Assessment 
through Formal Verification in HRC) aims at exploring all possible execution workflows, automatically 
identifying hazards in these workflows, and presenting mitigation strategies for these hazards that 
should be verified iteratively. In the presented methodology, the robot system is translated into a 
logical model in which the main components of such a system (operator, robot, layout) are modelled 
with certain characteristics. Next, formalised task descriptions are defined along a timeline in which 
the characteristics of the different components to achieve a certain task are described. This could, for 
example, be certain types of movements of the operator as well as the robot in certain parts of the 
described layout. This information is used to analyse the task workflow with regard to hazards by using 
a predefined set of possible hazardous situations. One example would be a hazard in which both 
human and operator are in the same part of the layout at the same time. Based on other 
characteristics of the modelled components like physical characteristics or the movement speeds, the 
risks are estimated in an automatically. Risk evaluation and reduction would afterwards be carried out 
by the system designer [50]. The semi-automated nature of the methodology could allow for 
execution of the verification while the system is running, which could allow turning risk mitigation 
measures on only when they are truly needed. The methodology can, however, be assumed to have 
limited applicability in practice, since it is lacking validation and since extensive modelling of the 
system is required in the first step. The modelling is hereby based on a modelling language (TRIO) that 
would need to be studied by industry professionals to be able to use the methodology. Furthermore, 
it is limited currently to the assessment of physical contact situations and the actual guidance on 
reducing the risks associated with these hazards is limited.  
A final limitation of the methodology, namely that foreseeable misuses of the system are not part of 
the assessment, is addressed by Askapour et al. In their work the authors extend the methodology to 
also include the most common erroneous behaviours of humans in the operator model that could help 
identify hazards that are otherwise overlooked [51]. This consideration for the human as an active 
actor through the inclusion of possible human errors builds a link to the next topic of interest, namely 
a socio-technical view/approach for the risk assessment of collaborative robot systems.  
==PAGE:37==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 29 
 
2.2.3 Action Theory and Socio-Technical View 
Initiated by the previously introduced work by Adriaensen et al. the following subchapter should 
further explore the socio-technical view on risk assessment and hazard analysis by also considering 
aspects of action theory. The core of socio-technical thinking is the consideration of both social as well 
as technical factors and the interactions between those. Changes in one part of the system will result 
in changes in other parts of the system, which is why all parts should be considered equally [52]. As 
mentioned before, the view on safety aspects of robot systems is currently techno-centric [10], [31] 
so the introduction of a socio-technical perspective builds a relevant extension for this work even 
though the consideration for socio-technical systems is not a new consideration, with works as early 
as 1960 by Emery and Trist [53]. 
To start extending the techno-centric viewpoint, one can consider human factors in collaborative 
robot systems. As presented in the work by Dobra and Dhir, human factors could include 
considerations for adaptation to new systems, leadership, trust in technology, human operator 
workload, or attitudes towards technical systems [28]. Such factors can also be observed in the 
elaborations concerning risk identification in this work (compare Chapter 2.2.1.1, Table 1). One could 
also consider safety engineering methods, or risk assessment methods that are based on a socio-
technical perspective. Huck et al. provide some introductory notes on possible methods that could fall 
in this category, like System Theoretic Process Analysis Method (STPA), Fault Tree Analysis (FTA), or 
Process Failure Mode and Effects Analysis (PFMA) [18].  
A more thorough and extensive introduction to possible methods that provide a socio-technical 
perspective is provided by Adriaensen et al. in [10] which should be considered as a core source for 
this subchapter and the following elaborations. In this work, Systems Theoretic Accident Model and 
Processes (STAMP), Functional Resonance Analysis Method (FRAM), and Event Analysis of Systemic 
Teamwork (EAST) are introduced [10].  
STAMP is an accident causality model which aims at finding adequate control strategies by analysing 
a system in the form of interrelated components which are kept in a state of equilibrium by control 
loops. These controls can be both in the form of human control, but also technical control. They are 
split into controllers and sub‐controllers, with control loops being the connection between the 
different levels. This allows to systematically plot the dependencies in answer to the question of how 
control and cognition are distributed. With this control structure, STAMP is the basis for the 
prospective hazard analysis conducted with the System Theoretic Process Analysis Method (STPA) 
which aims at developing safety constraints instead of just identifying inadequate control scenarios 
[10]. This, however, is, besides a short mention, outside the scope of this introductory explanation.  
Moving to the next method suggested in [10], FRAM is built around a description of functions, which 
represent any acts or activities. If necessary, the fundamental aspects of an activity can be described 
to extend the model (Input, Output, Requirements, Resources, Control, and Time). These aspects can 
==PAGE:38==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 30 
 
build links between different functions which produces a network description of the system. The 
FRAM framework analysis in the ensuing step “the potential for variability” [10, p. 10], i.e., the 
upstream and downstream effects of specific couplings between functions. To manage this variability 
positive effects should be enhanced while negative effects are dampened by for example inserting 
barriers, closing feedback loops, or rearranging the order of functions [10]. 
Finally, in [10] the EAST framework is introduced. It combines different tools in the analysis of a system 
in which interactions between humans and between human and artifacts are studied. First, different 
networks are developed (task, social, and information) and then evaluated in a network of networks. 
This analysis can either be quantitively by applying network analysis metrics or qualitatively. While the 
exact network metrics differ between different systems and domains, the centrality and 
connectedness of nodes and their relative distances to the other nodes are typically included in the 
analysis [10]. 
These introduced frameworks and methods from systems thinking inspired accident causation 
approaches could build the basis for a systematic hazard analysis approach. To extend the socio-
technical perspective one can also consider the concepts of Action Theory. In this, an action is defined 
by the agent who does the action, the act type, the modality of action (by what manner and by what 
means was the action done?), the setting/context of an action, and the rationale of an action [54]. 
With these aspects of action, one can build a comprehensive description of any kind of action. For 
interactions between humans and machines, the following four types of control actions can be 
described as the most relevant which are based on eight elementary action types described by Von 
Wright [55], [56]: 
• 
Steering: Ensure that a desired state is produced/reached. 
• 
Regulation: Ensure that a desired state is maintained/kept. 
• 
Tripping: Stop/End the current state of the system (e.g., the state is unsafe even if it is desired). 
• 
Interlocking: Ensure that an unsafe state is not produced/reached.  
The narrowing to four control types from eight elementary action types can be explained with the 
focus on interventions, i.e., actions where an agent needs to act. Omissions, i.e. actions where an 
agent intentionally does nothing and lets the system change itself, are not considered in the control 
actions since no actor is interfering with the system – whereas the control actions have the intention 
of modelling the interventions and interferences of different actors in the system [56].  
Tripping and Interlocking hereby are safety-related controls, whereas Steering and Regulation are only 
indirectly related to safety [56]. Each of the control actions has a possible counteraction, which 
constitutes the need for performing the control action and which could describe a possible failure that 
could occur in a system if the control function were to fail. For example, the counteraction for steering 
would be resisting or for regulation, it would be attempting.  
==PAGE:39==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 31 
 
Steering and Tripping as well as Regulation and Interlocking describe similar control actions. In one 
case to change the current state of a system and in the other case to maintain the current state. 
Nonetheless, the differentiation between the actions is vital for complete modelling since it allows to 
consider the agents' intention when modelling the control actions. While Steering and Tripping are 
concerned with reaching or maintaining a desired state, Regulation and Interlocking are concerned 
with leaving or rejecting an undesired state.  
Furthermore, besides the different control types as an aspect of actions, one can introduce the action 
phases. An action can be split into the phase of creating the possibility for an action to occur and the 
actualisation of that action. For the possibility of an action to exist the potentiality must be created 
during the design stage, for example by giving the actor the power to perform an action. Secondly, the 
opportunity for action must be considered during the operation of the system, for example by 
ensuring that the performing agent is ready for the action [57], [58]. 
If the possibility is given, an action can be executed in the first step of its actualisation through 
initiating and performing the actions. Finally, the action can be completed by the agent [57], [58]. 
Appendix 4 presents a schematic overview of these action phases for further study and consideration. 
With these phases, one can extend the descriptions to action hierarchies. Each action could, for 
example, be the condition for the execution of a higher action or it could trigger/actualize certain steps 
within the phases of the higher action.  
These basic concepts of action can be considered for a systematic hazard analysis methodology as 
described by Wu et al.[58]. In their work, the authors use means-end analysis and the action phases 
to create a model of a robot system that could be used for different phases during the system 
development or operation. Furthermore, the developed methodology is applied to a case study where 
a robot system is monitored based on the developed model. However, action theory could also be 
considered for a systematic analysis of hazards during the design stage [58]. 
2.3 Use-Cases for Collaborative Robot Systems 
Looking at actions and tasks performed by different actors of the human-robot collaborative system 
is, as explored, at the core of performing a thorough risk assessment for these systems. To be able to 
explore the actions and tasks, however, one first needs to build an understanding with regard to use-
cases and applications in which human-robot collaboration can be seen.  
One can observe different categories of use-cases [5], [7]–[9]: 
• 
Handling (Kitting, Packing, Pick and Place, Machine Tending) 
• 
Joining and Dispensing (Welding, Gluing, Sealing, Painting)  
• 
Assembly (Pre-assembly, Final Assembly, Screwdriving, Part Insertion) 
• 
Quality Inspection  
• 
Finishing/Material Removal 
==PAGE:40==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 32 
 
Similarly, in their study, Segura et al. categorised applications into four groups based on their analysis 
of case studies presented in academia [31]: 
• 
Assembly (Manual setting, Screwing, Mounting, Fixing) 
• 
Material Manipulation (Feeding, Positioning, Lifting, Picking/Placing) 
• 
Machining Processes (Milling, Drilling, Surface finishing) 
• 
Quality Inspection (Visual screening, Sensor screening) 
Noteworthy is that no use-cases in the category of joining and dispensing were observed by the 
authors during their study, besides that the overlap with the previously introduced categories is high. 
A majority of cases identified by Segura et al. fall into the category of assembly and material 
manipulation [31]. After being identified, the application groups are analysed with regard to their 
structural components, which in this case refer to interaction level, work role, communication level, 
and safety modes. The idea of interaction levels should be introduced in the following in connection 
with other works to allow for a more structured analysis of use-cases in the further elaborations.  
The first way of classifying interaction levels is used in different research, for example, both by Segura 
et al., as well as Dobra and Dhir [28], [31]: 
• 
Independent: Human and robot work independently on separate workpieces but are present 
in the same workspace.   
• 
Simultaneous: Human and robot work on separate processes on the same workpiece at the 
same time.   
• 
Sequential: Human and robot work on the same workpiece with sequential steps so that there 
is a time dependency between the two agents.   
• 
Supportive: Human and robot work towards the same process on the same workpiece through 
interaction.  
A similar classification is introduced by Arents et al. where the system description is based on the 
collaboration levels [29]: 
• 
Coexistence: Human and robot share a workspace but no processes/goals.  
• 
Cooperation: Human and robot share a goal and work together in shared spaces. 
• 
Collaboration: Human and robot work simultaneously on the same workpiece in the same 
space. 
The different descriptions for levels of collaboration in collaborative robot systems have also been 
studied and refined by Aaltonen et al. After consideration for nine different models/schematics to 
classify human-robot collaboration they conclude by proposing four levels of collaboration: No 
coexistence, i.e. physical separation; coexistence; cooperation; and collaboration [59]. These four 
levels, which have been extended compared to the levels described by Arents [29], will also be used 
for this work.  
==PAGE:41==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Situational Analysis 
Page 33 
 
No matter the applied schematic description, all collaborative robot systems have in common that 
they require specific considerations for the safety aspect of human operators. This safety aspect is 
often discussed in line with the operational modes as described by ISO/TS 15066 and as introduced in 
Chapter 2.1.4 of this work (SSM, HG, PFL). Therefore, the collaboration levels introduced in this 
chapter share some similarities with these operational modes While collaboration requires 
considerations for HG and PFL, cooperation is more reliant on PFL and SSM. Coexistence can likely 
efficiently be designed in a safe manner by considering SSM. The operational modes are not limited 
to the connection to interaction levels as described here but use-cases utilising all interaction levels 
can theoretically be designed with the application of all different operational models.  
The different classification levels, as well as the possible operational modes for use-cases for 
collaborative robot systems, should be kept in mind for the further elaborations of this work. It will, 
later on, allow for better classification and description of concrete use-cases.  
2.4 Limitations of the Current Situation  
To conclude the second chapter of this work some of the findings from the assessment of the current 
situation of hazard analysis for collaborative robot systems both from a theoretical as well as a 
practical point of view should be summarised in this subchapter.  
First, the practical state of the art was assessed through an introduction and discussion of harmonised 
norms. In this, one could observe that the information provided in the introduced norms is lacking. 
Methods in the identification and mitigation of hazards are limited to checklists and they are often 
presented without specific machines or use-cases in mind. The applicability for a specific use-case is 
challenging and requires experience from the machine designer. Furthermore, additional risk factors 
are not considered in these checklists. Even if specific guidance is provided, as with ISO/TS 15066 for 
collaborative robotics, applying these standards remains a challenge. This is both in the question of 
when to apply the different mitigation strategies presented, and also in the question of how these 
strategies must be validated afterward. The norms are, in addition, critiqued with regard to the 
contained information. This led to a revision of the existing standards where some operational modes 
for collaborative systems are excluded (SMS) and where the terminology concerning these systems 
changed (collaborative application and task instead of collaborative robot and workspace). 
Second, the state of research was assessed through a literature review. Safety aspects of human-robot 
collaboration were found to be a strong focus in ongoing research, however, the industrial 
practitioners´ views and needs are not always considered. This can be observed through the fact that 
existing norms and standards are often not considered for theoretical works. This gap results in novel 
methodologies that are not applicable to industrial settings because they are too complex, 
fragmented, or not validated. Furthermore, ongoing research is assessed to often be techno-centric. 
This is especially observed when hazard mitigation strategies are discussed in research. For example, 
the focus is on containing (physical interaction) energy, where the frequency of access to a 
==PAGE:42==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Goal 
Page 34 
 
collaborative space and the energy of the system (for example defined through the speed at which 
the robot moves) play key roles, which is insufficient in a system where human and robot need to 
collaborate as two actors in a socio-technical system. It is important to note and observe that new risk 
factors must be considered in collaborative robot systems (Chapter 2.2.1.1) and that new mitigation 
strategies for these factors must be applied (Chapter 2.2.1.2). This again shows the heightened 
complexity of the hazard analysis for collaborative systems which builds the impetus for this work. 
The need for novel, systematic methods for the risk assessment of collaborative systems that can deal 
with these new requirements is also recognised in research. Based on the task-planning, which is a 
core question in collaborative robotics (Chapter 2.2.2), or based on a socio-technical viewpoint, which 
considers all actors of the system (Chapter 2.2.3), new methods are developed or existing methods 
from other domains are applied to the field of collaborative robotics. However, there still is a need to 
find tools and methods that balance advanced analytic capabilities with ease of use for the system 
designer, so that the challenge of conducting a risk assessment for collaborative robot systems can be 
reduced for industry practitioners.  
For a better understanding of the landscape of collaborative robotics for industrial practitioners, some 
notes on use-cases for collaborative robot systems were also provided in this chapter. Noteworthy are 
the four levels of collaboration No coexistence; Coexistence; Cooperation; and Collaboration that 
should be considered for this work. This concludes the analysis of the current situation. The presented 
information should now be used to define specific goals for this work and to present the 
methodologies that should be followed to reach these goals.  
3 Goal 
In the next step of the systems engineering problem-solving cycle, which is the basis for the structure 
of this work, specific goals for the project must be defined. To be able to set specific goals and 
objectives that are based on a solid information base, the systems engineering problem-solving cycle 
suggests setting the goals of a project at the end of the situational analysis, and hereby at the end of 
the search for objectives. The results of the situational analysis should be used to set solution-neutral, 
completely operational, and realistic objectives. They may be split into mandatory, desired, and 
recommended objectives to establish priorities concerning the relative importance of the set 
objectives [21]. This allows for a better reflection and evaluation of the developed solution to end the 
project.  
The following chapter aims at introducing and describing the specific objectives for this project and 
the requirements for the solution that should be developed in the following. The subsequent 
elaborations will hereby be mainly based on the findings previously presented in Chapter 2.4. Firstly, 
the mandatory goals should be introduced. These are the goals that must be fulfilled so that the 
developed solution builds on the impetus that was discussed in Chapter 1. Most importantly, this 
project aims at developing a systematic approach for the hazard analysis for collaborative robot 
==PAGE:43==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Goal 
Page 35 
 
systems. This is the impetus for starting this project. This systematic approach should hereby consider 
the need to balance advanced capabilities and ease of use so that the tool can support inexperienced 
users. Furthermore, the goal is set for the approach to focus on the hazard identification stage (as well 
as secondary on the hazard mitigation) in the risk assessment process. These steps were found to be 
especially challenging for inexperienced users, so the limitation to these vital steps of the risk 
assessment has a solid foundation. It ensures a more narrow and more limited project scope so that 
a solution can realistically be developed according to the goals with the resource-related restrictions 
of this project in mind.  
Secondly, three desired goals are introduced. Fulfilling these aims would significantly improve the 
developed solution. For the solution of this project, it is desired to consider a Task-Based perspective 
as well as a Socio-Technical perspective for the newly developed approach. These perspectives were 
found to be value-adding to the risk assessment process in the situational analysis, specifically in the 
literature review (see Chapter 2.2). Furthermore, the approach should be practically usable and 
applicable for industrial users, again referring back to the impetus of supporting inexperienced users. 
Thirdly and finally, recommended goals complete the entire project scope. These could further 
improve the solution, however not fulfilling them would not significantly worsen the developed 
approach. This project aims at testing and validating the assumptions made in the development of the 
systematic approach. Furthermore, it aims at building a solution that addresses not only parts of the 
risk assessment procedure, i.e., that is not fragmented. While this aim seems to contradict the 
previous goal of focusing on the hazard analysis, the importance of the hazard identification and 
mitigation for the entire risk assessment process means that even though the focus lays on specific 
steps, still a comprehensive approach for the risk assessment can be developed. These recommended 
goals draw back to the previous findings of current research being fragmented and lacking validation. 
They elaborate on the desired goal of creating a practically usable approach. The goals, split into the 
three categories and building on the impetus, are summarised as follows in Figure 6: 
 
Figure 6: Project Goals (own figure)

Method

4 Method 
With the goal for this project defined, the next chapter introduces the methodology that is followed 
to develop a solution suited to reach the set objectives. While this approach is a slight deviation from 
the classic structure of the systems engineering problem-solving cycle [21], the inclusion of a chapter 
describing the used methodology in some more detail is deemed necessary for the scope of this 
scientific work. The main aim of the subsequent chapter and subchapters is hereby to introduce what 
will be done to reach the objectives and why the choices are made to follow these approaches. 
Specifically, the chapter will be split into subchapters to introduce the following:  
In the Overall Approach, the overarching structure for the following development of a solution will be 
described. Within this, the approach for developing an initial suggestion as well as the iterative process 
for improving on this initial suggestion will be introduced.  
In the Approach for the Iteration, the structure to be followed to improve the solution will be 
described. This includes the way more data shall be gathered as well as the purpose why more data is 
needed and why the chosen approach is suitable for the objective.  
Originally, a second iteration of the solution was planned. However, due to time-related constraints, 
this iteration had to be left out of the scope of this project. This will be reflected later on in Chapter 6.  
4.1 Overall Approach  
For developing the final solution, this project will follow an iterative process. First, an initial suggestion 
for a systematic approach for the hazard analysis for collaborative robot systems should be 
introduced. This first solution will be mainly based on the information gathered through the situational 
analysis, i.e., both the information gathered through the review of standards and norms, the literature 
review as well as the analysis of typical use-cases for collaborative robot systems.  
The initial solution should be focused on working towards the previously introduced mandatory goal 
of focusing on hazard identification and hazard mitigation. Furthermore, the first suggestion should 
also already consider the desired objectives of an approach based on a Task-Based and a Socio-
Technical perspective.  
This will be achieved by first continuing the analysis of typical use-cases through the study of publicly 
available video material. This will be introduced and referenced accordingly. These use-cases will be 
specifically analysed with regard to present hazards as well as used mitigation strategies. This 
information will be used to develop an initial suggestion for a systematic approach.  
Analysing use-cases, observing patterns and similarities, and based on this formulating a general 
conclusion in the form of a systematic method follows an inductive research approach where 
generalisations are drawn from specific observations. Even though some existing methods or theories 
from ongoing research exist, and testing these would be suitable for a deductive research approach, 
==PAGE:45==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 37 
 
this work aims at developing a novel systematic method for the hazard analysis for collaborative robot 
systems – making an inductive approach for this stage more suited. Existing research, the developed 
methods as well as the present findings should in this case be used as a further data source in the 
creation of an initial solution suggestion.  
4.2 Approach for the Iteration  
Iterating on the initial suggestion, the continued project strives to work towards the other previously 
described goals. Namely, that the solution should balance advanced capabilities with ease of use and 
that it should be practically usable for industrial robot users. To achieve this, more data must be 
gathered. Specifically, data related to the experiences and needs of industrial users of collaborative 
robot systems should be collected. For this purpose, interviews are deemed a suitable data gathering 
method. Rowley, for example, states that interviews are used for “gaining insights into or 
understanding of opinions, attitudes, experiences, processes, behaviours, or predictions” [60, p. 2], 
where this project mainly aims at learning more about opinions and experience of industry 
professionals. The exact methodology for the interview, as well as for the analysis of the data gathered 
through the interview, will be introduced together with the results in Chapter 5.2.  
Based on the findings and information collected through the interviews, the initial solution suggestion 
should be iterated on and improved to create a practically applicable tool for industrial users. With 
this, the second part of the thesis follows a more deductive research approach. An existing solution, 
or an existing theory, should be analysed. For this purpose, hypotheses are generated and tested 
through data collection. However, for this project, the deductive stage of research is limited to the 
aforementioned interviews and therefore conclusions drawn about the hypotheses have to be 
considered carefully. This will be reflected to conclude this work.  
5 Solution 
The original structure of the problem-solving cycle suggests creating different solution concepts, 
evaluating them, and choosing between them. This chapter will rather focus on the iterative 
development of one solution concept. This will still reflect the main ideas behind the problem-solving 
cycle, namely to look for the best and most appropriate way of reaching the set objectives [21]. The 
iterative approach is hereby best suited for this scientific work, and it will in the next subchapters 
follow the structure and the approach outlined previously in Chapter 4. Herein, the subsequent 
chapter of this thesis aims at working towards the specified objectives as seen in Chapter 3, while 
observing the objective hierarchy that was defined as well. Specifically, the following aims at 
developing a systematic approach for the hazard analysis for collaborative robot systems that balances 
advanced capabilities and ease of use and that focuses on the hazard identification and the hazard 
mitigation stages of the risk assessment process.  
==PAGE:46==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 38 
 
5.1 Initial Suggestion 
A customer building his or her collaborative robot application might have limited knowledge with 
regard to building different applications, CE certification, or specific collaborative operational modes. 
What is known is the application that the customer wants to build. Therefore, the solution to be 
developed should be based on typical use-cases and guide the user through the process based on the 
specific type of use-case. This allows for the solution that is to be developed to be practically applicable 
by focusing on ease of use for the end customer. Furthermore, by focusing on the use-case in the first 
step of the solution, a task-based perspective is already considered for the solution. To prepare this 
approach, the next subchapter analyses different use-cases for collaborative robot systems. This 
analysis does not aim to cover all possible applications and use-cases but rather the most common 
ones to allow the broadest possible coverage.  
5.1.1 Analysis of Typical Use-Cases  
As introduced in Chapter 2.3, different use cases for collaborative robot systems exist. For this work, 
based on gathered theoretical and practical information, the following five categories of use-cases 
should be used:  
• 
Handling (Kitting, Packing, Pick and Place, Machine Tending) 
• 
Joining and Dispensing (Welding, Gluing, Sealing, Painting) 
• 
Assembly (Pre-assembly, Final Assembly, Screwdriving, Part Insertion) 
• 
Quality Inspection  
• 
Finishing/Material Removal 
These categories introduce and describe the most important types of use cases. They should now be 
analysed with regard to the collaboration level as introduced in Chapter 2.3 as well as certain safety 
aspects. For this purpose, video case studies published on the official Universal Robots YouTube 
channel will serve as a source. After assessing the videos for applicability, it was decided to add one 
more use-case type to the handling group, namely palletising. This is because palletising seems to 
receive a strong focus in the presented case studies. The results of the analysis of these use-cases are 
summarised in Table 2 below. Of each use-case type, two to three videos are considered for a first 
analysis. This is not a sufficient amount of data for a detailed analysis of these use-cases and general 
conclusions, however, the aim of this analysis is rather a first introduction and familiarisation with the 
different groups of use-cases so that they can confidently be used in the ensuing steps of this work.  
Besides the collaboration levels, which will be categorised according to the previously introduced four-
step scale, hazards and hazard mitigation should be described for each use-case. The most hazardous 
part of the collaborative robot system hereby refers to physical hazards that are present. They could 
be related to the tool, the workpiece, the robot's movement, the surroundings, and attached 
machines, or other hazards which could lead to injuries. The analysis of the present hazards does not 
aim at creating a complete risk assessment but rather just to note the most relevant hazards that can 
==PAGE:47==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 39 
 
be observed for the type of use-case with the limited information which is presented in the videos. 
The same holds for the discussion on hazard mitigations, in which not all used mitigation strategies 
should be presented and analysed but rather just those that seem noteworthy from the information 
presented in the videos or that are specific to the type of use-case. A further limitation of this first 
analysis is the limited amount of information which is provided through the videos. Therefore, the 
analysis is more or less detailed for the different use-cases depending on the extent to which the 
aspects were discussed and/or shown in the videos.
