==PAGE:50==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 42 
 
The first conclusion after the conducted analysis is the focus on lower levels of collaboration. 
Cooperation, hereby mostly sequential, and coexistence are strongly dominating in the assessed use-
cases while collaboration as the highest level is not observed. This conforms to the previous finding, 
as also described in [10], [11]. For the given data samples this finding holds over all different use-case 
groups. One limitation of this assessment must now however be mentioned and explained to set the 
results in context. The videos used and referenced to create Table 2 are published by Universal Robots 
or by partner companies of Universal Robots. Therefore, they must be assessed as marketing material 
with a focus on positively presenting the robotic arms. This might be a reason that the lowest level of 
collaboration, i.e., no collaboration, is rarely observed in the applications. This might be a sampling 
bias and in actual industrial practice, the share of applications that are solved without any 
collaboration could be larger.  
The described limitation has a further implication for this work. The focus of marketing material 
published by Universal Robots is likely on highlighting the message that should be conveyed by the 
company, namely that its robots are easy to use and quick to set up. Therefore, the shown applications 
mostly do not highlight any hazard mitigation strategies or safety functions/safety components that 
are used in the system. It could be that these discussions are deliberately left out of the videos. Or it 
could be that only those applications are chosen to be presented in which no additional safety 
components have been installed. This is because the addition of safety components complicates and 
elongated the set-up of the collaborative robot system, which does not fit with the marketing 
message. In line with this limitation, nearly no applications are shown where human and robot are 
physically separated through the use of safety fences, which again can refer back to the choice of 
applications presented as marketing material on the official Universal Robots YouTube channel.  
Nonetheless, the analysed use-case videos show collaborative robot systems from industrial practice, 
so the discussion of these applications is still valuable for this work through extending the theoretical 
analysis of the topic with considerations from industrial practice. Therefore, the following two 
subchapters will summarize the findings with regard to typical hazards as well as with regard to typical 
mitigation strategies to set the results into context for the further elaborations of this work. 
5.1.1.1 
Analysis of Typical Hazards  
Before a discussion of the hazards observed in the use-case videos, it must be stated that the analysis 
in this work is performed by the author, so personal biases can not be avoided. In the analysis of typical 
hazards, this could result in a stronger focus on certain hazard groups while others are disregarded. In 
addition, the analysis of hazards does not, as mentioned previously, aim at finding and assessing all 
relevant hazards but just at discussing some of the most obvious hazards of the presented use-cases.  
Drawing back to the analysis of Chapter 2.2.1.1, most hazards identified from the limited data 
provided by the videos can be categorised in risk group 2 (Technology) or 3 (Collaborative Workspace). 
Within these groups, the cobot system and the physical design of the workspace are where most of 
==PAGE:51==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 43 
 
the observed hazards can be placed. This can be explained by the fact that hazards are always 
identified regarding possible physical injuries to the human operator and these two categories are the 
most likely to present risks where the consequence is physical injury. Furthermore, the focus on these 
categories can be explained by the limited available data which makes an assessment of other risk 
groups infeasible.  
For the cobot system, by far the most hazardous system part that is noted in the analysis of use-case 
videos is the gripper or the tool at the end of arm of the robotic arm. Physical contact with the tool 
could often potentially lead to injury to the human operator. This could especially be observed with 
specially built end effectors, where sharp edges and corners are not rounded or padded so that the 
contact with this system part could be harmful to the operator. It could further be observed in tools 
that have a sharp tip to be able to work towards the process goal, like dispensing tools or screwdriving 
tools. Also in welding applications is the end effector observed to be the most hazardous part of the 
system.  
The same can be noted for the workpiece handled by the robot. Sharp edges or corners can not always 
be avoided so that physical contact with the workpiece might lead to injury. Risks are especially 
observed in cases where a clamping situation with the workpiece might occur like in a part insertion 
or handling application where the part is placed in an area accessible to the human operator.  
Finally, as part of the collaborative system, the robot arm´s movements are assessed to be hazardous 
in some use-cases. This is noteworthy for cases in which the robotic arm is mounted high or where 
the arm is mounted on the ceiling so that movements could occur in the head area of the human 
operator. Further, the movements are assessed to be hazardous when the end of arm needs to move 
in a straight line, for example in a polishing application. Straight-line movement of the tool could lead 
to unexpected movements of other parts of the robot arm like the shoulder, which could lead to a 
hazardous contact situation with the human operator.  
To summarize, hazards in the cobot system are mostly observed for potential physical contact 
situations with different parts of the robot system, either related to the tool, to the workpiece, or to 
movements of the robot which are unintuitive for the human operator. For all these contact situations 
is, as mentioned previously, the combination of the energy of the contact and frequency of access to 
the hazardous area vital for the comprehensive risk assessment.  
Hazards in the collaborative workspace that are observed in the use-cases were on the one hand in 
relation to surrounding machines. This could be CNC machines that present hazards or clamps used 
to fixate workpieces for the following process steps. On the other hand, hazards in the environment 
could be again in relation to physical contact situations, for example, a clamping situation where part 
of the human body is clamped between the robot system and the environment. In this case, sharp 
edges present in the environment could lead to serious injury to the human operator.  
==PAGE:52==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 44 
 
5.1.1.2 
Analysis of Typical Mitigation 
Similar to the analysis of typical hazards, the discussion regarding the mitigation strategies observed 
in the video case studies is again restricted by the limited available data, the sampling bias of analysing 
marketing material, as well as by the personal bias of the author. This could result in a 
nonrepresentative analysis of mitigation strategies as well as an analysis that sets a certain focus. 
Nonetheless, this chapter aims to introduce some of the mitigation strategies used in the case studies.  
Firstly, risks are reduced by the design of the collaborative robot system. On the one hand, this can be 
achieved through specific collaborative operational modes like PFL. This is regularly observed in the 
video-case studies. Noteworthy are two case studies in which the PFL function of the robot is tested 
with human test subjects [61], [67], which is not advisable and which should not be copied. 
Furthermore, SSM is observed in some case studies through the addition of laser range scanners and 
HG is observed through the addition of specific HG switches at the end of arm tool of the robot. 
However, the clear focus within the operational modes is on PFL. On the other hand, risk reduction by 
design is achieved through considerations for the layout of the collaborative robot systems. Access to 
hazardous areas of the layout is removed through specific placement of the robot, walls, surrounding 
machines or shelves, or other elements of the environment in which the robot is placed. This can be 
a way of removing hazards from a system without the need for additional safety fencing and without 
limiting the efficiency and effectiveness of the system.  
Secondly, risks are observed to be reduced through safeguarding. This could be the addition of light 
curtains. It could also be through the use of special collaborative grippers, which are limited in their 
gripping force, and which have rounded corners and edges. In some cases, sharp edges are also 
removed with covers or padding. Thirdly, and finally, in some use-cases, complementary measures 
like PPE and specific considerations for providing information of use could be observed. The clear focus 
in the analysed case studies is however on risk reduction through the use of PFL and through working 
with the layout/environment of the collaborative robot system.  
This focus is, as mentioned, not necessarily representative of all industrial applications. Nonetheless, 
reducing the risk of potential mechanical hazards that could occur in a contact situation between 
human and robot through lowering the interaction energy by utilising PFL (reducing the speed of the 
robot) or reducing the frequency in which the hazardous situation might occur is in line with the 
previous finding that these two factors are among the most important for deciding on a suitable 
mitigation strategy [32] and with the risk reduction strategy described in ISO 12100 [22].  
In addition to these strategies observed in the use-cases, one could argue for additional measures for 
risk reduction. Important to mention here are specific strategies for programming the robot 
movements since movements in the operator's head region or unintuitive movements of the robot 
arm were found to be among the most relevant hazards. For example, one could perform the 
programming of the robot through HG where the operator physically moves the tool to the different 
==PAGE:53==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 45 
 
waypoints, hereby intuitively avoiding dangerous areas around their head and making the movements 
better understandable and more transparent. For any HG operation, however, an additional enabling 
switch for the application should be considered, for example placed directly at the tool. Another 
strategy that should again be mentioned is the use of physical characteristics of the robot system to 
reduce potential hazards by reducing the injury severity. While this strategy is observed in some of 
the use-cases, many more could have benefitted. Especially those in which a specially built gripper is 
being used, since those gripping systems are often observed to have sharp and hazardous edges.  
5.1.2 Systematic Hazard Analysis 
After the analysis of use-cases as well as the discussion of hazards and hazard mitigation strategies 
observed in these, the initial suggestion for a systematic hazard analysis for collaborative robot 
systems should in the following be introduced. This will, besides the analysis of typical use-cases, be 
based on the carried-out literature review. As stated in the introduction to this chapter, the basis for 
the systematic process are the typical use-cases for collaborative robot systems. This allows customers 
to be guided through the process based on what is known to them, namely the task to be carried out 
by the robotic system. The applications used in the systematic process are the ones introduced and 
analysed in the previous subchapter. It has to be noted that these applications do not cover the entire 
spectrum of possible use-cases for collaborative robot systems, however, the most important and 
common ones – based on previous research [5], [7]–[9] – are included. Use-cases outside of these five 
categories always require special and thorough considerations in the risk assessment which is why 
they are not included in the systematic process. The aim is not to reach complete coverage, but rather 
to make it as easy as possible for the included applications.  
To make it as easy as possible, the main concept of the proposed schematic is the availability of 
different exit points in the process. The system designer should be made aware as soon as possible if 
it is not desirable, efficient, or possible to solve the application with the use of a collaborative 
operational mode. This reduces the effort needed in the cases in which a collaborative system design 
is not possible. Without the use of the proposed process, this conclusion could only be drawn after 
preparing the entire risk assessment of the system. The systematic allows guidance for the designer 
at an earlier stage of the design process. Nonetheless, it has to be mentioned that the systematic 
process does not relieve the designer of the responsibility of conducting a thorough risk assessment 
of the system. This is still needed for certification and safe use of the system. The systematic only aims 
at removing complexity and effort from this process.  
To this end, the first question or decision the designer is guided through is whether the design of a 
collaborative application is actually desirable. The extent and the frequency in which the human 
operator needs to access the potential collaborative workspace are core considerations in this 
decision. If access is only needed seldom and/or for short intervals at a time it might not be worth 
designing the application collaboratively. As mentioned, the combination of frequency of access and 
==PAGE:54==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 46 
 
energy of interaction is a core consideration in the design of a collaborative system. Therefore, the 
second question aims at finding out whether the design of the collaborative application is made 
infeasible based on the energy of the system. Factors playing into the (interaction) energy are the tool, 
workpiece, and the cycle time of the application. Heavy tools and workpieces likely make the 
application infeasible for collaborative operational modes, as the hazards and potential injuries 
related to this could not be avoided. The same holds for applications in which sharp edges or corners 
are present that can not be covered or changed. In addition, high required or desired cycle times make 
a collaborative design infeasible as the speed at which the robot needs to move would be too high, 
resulting in interaction energy that is too high.  
If the design of a collaborative operational mode is both desired by the designer as well as possible 
based on considerations regarding the interaction energy, there are still other factors that might make 
the use of such operational modes infeasible. Namely, those applications in which hazardous materials 
are being used or processed might need to be physically separated from the human operator. 
Furthermore, certain movements of the robot might make a collaborative design impossible. 
Specifically, this refers to movements of the robot in the head region of the human operator which is 
prohibited according to the current standards. This case could especially occur when the robot is 
mounted relatively high or when it is mounted from the ceiling. In these situations, human and robot 
would not be allowed to move in the same workspace at the same time. This could result in the 
discission to design an application without a collaborative element.  
In the case that collaboration is both desired as well as possible based on the energy of interaction 
and other factors, two different options for collaborative operational modes that can be utilised in the 
system design exist for the designer. Namely, Power and Force Limiting (PFL) or Speed and Separation 
Monitoring (SSM) allow for safe operation in shared workspaces. To differentiate between the two, 
again the interaction energy is a deciding factor. Low interaction energy (low speeds, low weight, etc.) 
suits PFL while high interaction energy (higher speeds, higher weights) would suit SSM. Applications 
with movements in the head region of a human operator build an exception to this rule. They would, 
even with low interaction energy, only be suited to SSM since no synchronised movements of human 
and robot would be possible. The suggestion of the systematic process to build an application utilising 
either PFL or SSM builds a possible endpoint of the systematic process. At this endpoint, the designer 
is met with further suggestions for the continued system design. As mentioned, following the 
described process still a detailed and thorough risk assessment of the entire application/system is 
needed. In this risk assessment, further risk reduction must be applied, and depending on the chosen 
collaborative operational mode different strategies for reducing risks exist.  
For PFL a core consideration for reducing risks could be the layout of the application. Reducing the 
shared workspace and moving more hazardous robot operations out of the shared space can mitigate 
risks related to the application. Furthermore, the application designer might need to lower the speed 
==PAGE:55==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 47 
 
at which the robot moves to be able to keep the interaction energy within acceptable thresholds. A 
further measure for energy reduction could be considerations for physical characteristics of the 
system, for example removing sharp edges and corners through energy absorbent padding. In 
addition, the designer should take care in designing and specifying the robot's movements to make 
them as transparent as possible to the operator. Unintuitive robot movement can lead to hazardous 
situations. Finally, in PFL applications, the use of tools and grippers designed for collaborative 
operation is advised. This can reduce risks related to specially built tooling.  
For SSM the different strategies suggested for PFL remain applicable. However, additional safety 
components must be included in the system design. These could be light fences or laser range scanners 
that slow down and potentially stop the robot's movements once a human operator is in the vicinity 
of the robot. Furthermore, the specific robot arm placement can be used to reduce risks. One addition 
to any application designed for use with collaborative operational modes are considerations for other 
parts of the complete system that might be more hazardous than the robot itself. For example, a CNC 
machine in a machine tending application might create hazards that greatly exceed those present in 
the robotic part of the system. The same could hold for other system elements, like presses or clamps. 
Access to those, more hazardous, elements of the complete system must always be removed so that 
the human operator can not reach into the hazard zones created by these elements.  
If the system designer takes any of the exit points of the systematic process, i.e. the designer decides 
that a collaborative system design is either not desired or not possible due to the interaction energy 
or due to other factors, the process would lead to a different endpoint. This endpoint suggests that 
the automatic mode of the collaborative robot system is designed without collaboration. The 
automatic mode hereby is defined as a “control state that allows executing programmed tasks” [20, 
Ch. 3]. Nonetheless, if desired by the designer, a collaborative operation would still be possible even 
though the automatic mode is designed with physical separation of the different actors. In a manual 
mode, or a “control state that allows for the direct control by an operator” [20, Ch. 3], a Hand Guiding 
(HG) operational mode could be used for teaching the robot or for operator assistance. Noteworthy 
is that the distinction between these operational modes is not part of the currently applicable 
standard ISO 10218-2:20211 [26] but it has been included in the revised version ISO 10218-2:2020 
[20], showing the relevance of considering the difference between such modes.  
The inclusion of HG in the application design requires specific considerations for reducing risks during 
the risk assessment. On the one hand, the operator must at all times be aware of the robot system 
state or mode. Access during automatic mode must be avoided through physical separation or light 
fences while access is desired in HG operational modes. In addition, a HG activation device should be 
part of the system design. This can ensure that risk is reduced through a lowered possibility of the 
operator having body parts in hazardous areas and through a better possibility of avoidance of the 
hazard. The addition of HG is depended on the actual task of the robot system. The usefulness of the 
==PAGE:56==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 48 
 
application might be improved through the addition, even though it must be noted that the effort that 
must be spent during system design is heightened. A practical example to illustrate the argument of 
the usefulness of HG would be a welding application in which the human operator first teaches the 
robot system the points that must be welded together before the robot moves according to these 
taught points.  
Generally speaking, welding as an application for collaborative robot systems constitutes a small 
exemption to the presented systematic process. This is because the tool of the welding application, 
i.e., the welding torch, creates new hazards that need special consideration. These are thermal and 
radiation hazards that need to be considered by the system designer. If the design of a collaborative 
system is still desired, the use of PPE can not be avoided. Furthermore, people walking past the 
application must be protected through screens or opaque foils. The same holds for sanding 
applications, in which the tool again might create hazardous and harmful situations. Specifically, flying 
parts and high rotational speeds of the tool create new hazards that need special consideration both 
for personnel working in close vicinity but also for people walking past the application.  
Some are not been specific considerations in the presented process. The proposed systematic focused 
on physical hazards as these need more consideration for collaborative robot applications. Electrical 
hazards are, however, likely present in all applications. While these are not specifically mentioned in 
the systematic process, they still need to be considered in the complete risk assessment which must 
follow after the proposed process.  
The entire systematic process is presented in Figure 7 below. A constraint set for this graphic overview 
is that it should fit within one page, making it more accessible and creating a better overview.  
==PAGE:57==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 49 
 
 
Figure 7: Systematic Hazard Analysis for Collaborative Robot Systems (own figure) 
==PAGE:58==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 50 
 
5.2 Iteration  
The presented solution should in the next step be iterated on and improved on by a continued data 
gathering and data analysis process. As mentioned in Chapter 4.2, expert interviews are deemed an 
appropriate tool for gathering relevant data for this project. Hereby, qualitative research should reveal 
individual experiences and opinions with regard to the safety aspects of collaborative robot systems. 
This is needed since the risk assessment and the hazard analysis, as described previously, are highly 
dependent on the experience and knowledge of the individual conducting the risk assessment, as also 
defined by other researchers [13], [18]. Therefore, the expert interviews that are conducted as part 
of this work should strive towards gathering information on these experiences. The process for 
collecting expert data through interviews will be described in the next subchapter, before analysing 
the gathered data, and finally adapting the solution based on the findings.  
5.2.1 Data Gathering  
Semi-structured interviews are the chosen methodology for the data gathering process. The purpose 
of conducting semi-structured interviews is to keep a general structure to the interview that allows 
for comparability of the results of different conversations, while at the same time allowing the 
interviewer to flexibly react and adapt to the responses of the interviewee. Hereby, important aspects 
can be discussed in more detail and aspects that are not as important in individual interviews can be 
adapted and shortened [93], [94]. 
An interview guide is set up to help structure the conducted interviews and ensure that relevant 
information is gathered [95]. This guide will be introduced in the following subchapter. Within the 
data gathering process, the interview guide was used to facilitate twelve interviews. These were 
conducted between the 6th of April 2022 and the 2nd of June 2022. The interview participants as well 
as the process for inquiring about an interview will be introduced in the subsequent subchapter.  
5.2.1.1 
Interview Guide  
For the development of the interview guide, the framework suggested by Kallio et al. [96] is followed. 
Therefore, in the first step, the prerequisites for using a semi-structured interview must be identified. 
This was argued previously, on the one hand arguing for interviews as a data-gathering method 
through discussing the need of collecting data related to industrial practitioners' experiences and 
knowledge. On the other hand, arguing for semi-structured interviews through the combination of 
being able to flexibly adapt to the interviewee while ensuring comparability of the results. In the 
second step of development, previous knowledge should be retrieved, which has been done through 
Chapter 2 of this work. In the actual development of the guide, a preliminary guide should be 
formulated and tested before presenting the complete guide [96]. This structure is followed for this 
work. Testing of the guide is hereby done with other researchers, which would be referred to as expert 
testing by Kallio et al. [96]. 
==PAGE:59==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 51 
 
The final guide follows suggestions by both Kallio et al. [96] and Nelson [97]. Hereby, interview 
questions should be formulated open-ended, should not be leading the participant to a certain 
answer, and should be clearly worded [96]. To derive such questions, first general research questions 
are formulated that should be answered through the interviews [97]. For this work, the following 
hypotheses/assumptions were formulated for that purpose:  
• 
Underlying: There is an actual need for truly collaborative robot systems even though 
currently mainly co-existence is observed.  
• 
A1: The risk assessment process is more complex from an analytical standpoint for 
collaborative systems. Therefore, people need support for the hazard analysis for 
collaborative robot systems on an analytical level. 
• 
A2: There are typical hazards and typical mitigation strategies in collaborative systems. Most 
often, the tool or workpiece are the most relevant hazard sources when only considering the 
robot system.  
• 
A3: Currently, in the analysis, not all hazard sources are considered equally. Some phases of 
the life cycle and hazards from the broader work system receive little attention. 
First, an underlying assumption is defined whether there is a need for collaborative systems within 
the higher levels of collaboration. This is defined on the one hand to further explore the current 
landscape of collaborative robot use-cases since in the previous analysis mostly lower levels of 
collaboration have been observed. The assumption is on the other hand also defined to validate the 
impetus of this work, since the need for collaborative robot systems is a prerequisite for a systematic 
approach supporting the hazard analysis for these systems. Second, the assumption is made that 
people struggle with certain parts of the risk assessment process for collaborative robot systems. 
Answering this assumption would lead to knowing whether there is a need for a new, systematic 
approach for the hazard analysis – which is developed as part of this work. Third, the assumption 
should be tested that typical hazards and typical mitigation strategies for these hazards exist. These 
build the basis for the systematic process for hazard analysis that was introduced previously. Fourth, 
and finally, an assumption should test the previous finding that the view on risk assessment is techno-
centric and that it is limited to certain parts and life phases of the machine in question.  
With these assumptions, the final guide should be developed. The logical flow of the questions is 
hereby a core consideration [97]. The interview should start with questions that are easy to answer, 
and that help build a rapport between interviewer and interviewee, before leading to more difficult 
to answer questions in the end. The final question of the interview should provide some closure to 
the interview so that the interviewee is glad to have participated in the interview [97]. 
To achieve this logical flow in the interviews conducted for this work, the guide was structured into 
four sections as follows:  
 
==PAGE:60==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 52 
 
• 
Section 1/introduction, five minutes, background and personal experience 
• 
Section 2, ten minutes, previous risk assessments, and current process 
• 
Section 3, ten minutes, collaborative systems and foundation for improvements 
• 
Section 4/closing, five minutes, final thoughts 
Section 1 aims at creating a rapport between the interviewer and interviewee. First by introducing the 
scope and purpose of the research and second by asking about the participant's background and 
experience. Section 2 in the following is structured around finding out more about the risk assessment 
process in general. Topics include: what process does the interviewee follow; which tools are being 
used; how do they identify hazards; and how do they decide on mitigation strategies. Continuing, 
section 3 moves to collaborative robot systems. Questions aim at finding out more about collaborative 
robot applications/use-cases and the hazards as well as hazard mitigation strategies of these 
applications. Finally, section 4 closes the interview mainly by asking whether the participant wants to 
add anything that has not yet been mentioned as part of the questioning. The focus, as can be 
observed by the allocated time of each section, is on section 2 and section 3 which aim at gathering 
data useful for analysing the previously introduced assumptions. Section 1 and section 4 mainly serve 
as an introduction and as closure to the interview. The participant is hereby not made aware of the 
sections and the rough allocated times. This structure is only so that the interviewer can keep an 
overview of the interview process and allow for the interview to keep within the time of 30 minutes. 
The final guide including all questions is presented in Appendix 6.  
With the development of the guide, the interviews were defined to last around 30 minutes. This 
decision was taken to allow for conversations and discussions that result in new findings and 
interesting discoveries, while at the same time not asking the interviewee for an unreasonable amount 
of time. If the effort, i.e. the time the interviewee would have to invest, would be too high, the 
apprehension was that not enough participants could be identified. With the 30-minute interviews, 
the aim was to gather a dataset that is large enough to be able to draw general conclusions. This will 
be described in more detail in the next subchapter.  
5.2.1.2 
Interview participants  
In following the rule of thumb proposed by Rowley, as part of this thesis 12 interviews, each of around 
30 minutes in length are conducted [60]. This allows for a dataset that is large enough to be able to 
draw conclusions about the assumptions while at the same time not “drowning in a sea of data” [60, 
p. 4]. In addition, 30 minutes was deemed an appropriate timeframe that potential participants would 
be willing to make available for the interviews.  
For choosing interview participants, two factors are relevant. First, the profile of the interviewee, and 
second, the possibility of accessing the potential participant. With regard to the profile, the interviews 
should be conducted with people experienced in the field of integrating collaborative robot systems. 
They should have constructed and designed different systems, and they should be experienced with 
==PAGE:61==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 53 
 
conducting risk assessments, ideally also for collaborative robot systems. Therefore, the most 
important considerations for choosing participants are the current position and technical background.  
To be able to access people that fit within this category, people working at system integrators for 
collaborative robot systems are the target group for inquiries. Since this work is developed in 
cooperation with Universal Robots, accessible system integrators are the partner companies of UR. 
These are available on the Universal Robots website [98]. To find suitable candidates for interviews 
working at these companies, the company website or LinkedIn profile is consulted to try and find 
people working within the area of safety/CE certification/risk assessment of collaborative robot 
systems. Otherwise, project managers or in other cases managing personnel was reached out to 
regarding the possibility of joining an interview.  
To limit the scope of inquiring for interviews, partner companies from the DACH region (Germany, 
Austria, Switzerland), the Benelux region (Belgium, Netherland, Luxembourg), and Scandinavia 
(Denmark, Norway, Sweden) were considered. This also allows for better comparability of the results, 
since all partner companies are then subject to similar rules and regulations stemming from the 
requirements present in the European Union (e.g., a CE marking following the machine directive).  
With the profile and potential accessibility to participants described, in total 79 inquiries were sent to 
different companies/people working at these companies. The e-mail template used for this campaign 
is presented in Appendix 7. In total, a response rate of 37% (29 responses) was achieved. The positive 
response rate was 15% (12 people agreed to an interview) and 22% of the sent e-mails gathered a 
negative response (17 responses). The negative responses were hereby mostly related to missing 
time-related resources of the potential participant. Some negative responses also resulted from an 
initial agreement to an interview but in the subsequent communication, no agreement for a meeting 
could be found. A further reason why participants did not agree to an interview was missing 
experience in the area of risk assessment in system integration of collaborative robot systems. Some 
companies that were included in the campaign were just recently added to the list of certified system 
integrators, so they did not see themselves as suited to participate in the research interview.  
One negative response to the campaign stood out as the person said: “the hazards vary from project 
to project, one cannot generalise. We are therefore not available for an interview”6. While the person 
did not agree to an interview, their response already directly contradicts assumption A2.  
The twelve positive responses to the e-mail inquiry led to twelve interviews that were, as stated, 
conducted in the period between the 6th of April and the 2nd of June. All interviews were conducted 
digitally via Microsoft Teams. The following Table 3 shows an overview of the conducted interviews.

"Die Gefährdungen sind von Projekt zu Projekt unterschiedlich, kann man nicht verallgemeinern. Wir stehen für 
ein Interview daher nicht zu Verfügung."
