The data shown in Table 3 is anonymised. The name and company are left out for example, as they 
are not relevant for this work. The same will continue throughout the analysis of interview data where 
all gathered data will be used anonymously. This is in line with GDPR requirements for the record and 
processing of personal data. All twelve interviews were recorded digitally to which all participants 
agreed with written consent. The template used to gather consent for the processing of personal data 
is presented in Appendix 8 of this work. In addition, the Record of Processing Activities is presented in 
Appendix 9. Included in this record are information related to the type of personal data collected 
during the interviews, the storage of that personal data as well as responsible people for the personal 
data. The documentation was prepared in line with information published in [99] and it was reviewed 
by the responsible GDPR coordinator at the Technical University of Denmark. 
The interviews were recorded to be able to focus on the conversation, to be able to better analyse the 
data and to be able to present additional information to the following analysis. Disadvantages to 
recording interviews, for example stated in [97], do not apply to this project. The social encounter was 
not majorly altered through the recording since all interviews were conducted digitally and the time-
consuming transcription process was made more efficient through the availability of a tool to 
automatically transcribe the recordings (EnjoyHQ). These transcriptions are presented in the appendix 
(Appendix 10 to Appendix 21) of this work and will be referenced throughout the ensuing analysis. 
One interview has to be specifically mentioned since the interview was conducted in German which is  
==PAGE:63==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 55 
 
the mother tongue of the author. This was requested by the interview participant who did not feel 
comfortable speaking on the topic in English but preferred to speak in his mother tongue as well. For 
this interview, both the German transcript as well as the English translation of that transcript are 
presented in Appendix 17. This concludes the description of the data gathering process. In the 
following, the data gathered in this way should be analysed and discussed in more detail.  
5.2.2 Data Analysis  
As suggested by Rowley [60], the first step of the data analysis begins immediately after the interviews. 
Noteworthy findings, major discussion topics, and main themes of the interviews are written down 
while reviewing the recording of the interview. This allows a better familiarisation with the gathered 
data. The continued data analysis then aims to conduct a thematic analysis, i.e. to discover and link 
key themes emerging in the research into a coherent narrative. This analysis is performed after all 
interviews have been conducted. For this purpose, first, the data must be organised [60]. In this case, 
the interviews are uploaded and organised in the digital tool EnjoyHQ. Secondly, it is necessary to get 
acquainted with the data with a structured reading [60]. In this project, this included identifying and 
structuring interview participant quotes that help answer one of the previously defined assumptions. 
Since this is the focus of the analysis, a limitation of this work is that information from the interviews 
not relating to the assumptions will not be explored in depth. Therefore, in the case of this project, 
only four groups of analysis were identified, corresponding to the underlying and the three formulated 
assumptions. This process of turning data into small discrete components and adding these 
components to descriptive labels is also described as coding [100]. Continuing, themes and clusters of 
quotes must be formulated to interpret the data [60]. Finding connections between the individual 
quotes and hereby discovering different categories is also referred to as axial coding [100].  
The interview data is introduced and written up in the following sections by reporting on the themes 
and sub-themes that were identified for each assumption. With this, the interview analysis will 
continue with selective coding which connects the different identified themes [100]. Four subchapters 
representing the four assumptions will follow this introductory chapter to the data analysis. In these 
subchapters, interview quotes will be presented to illustrate the themes and findings where 
applicable. To allow the reader to get acquainted with the context which the quote was taken from, 
the interview transcripts that are presented in the appendix will be referenced in the following format: 
(Interview 1; [00:00:00]). The brackets refer to the timestamp at which the quote can be located in 
the complete transcription. This allows for easier navigation through the dataset. A summarising 
chapter will conclude the data analysis in which an overview over the conclusions as well as an 
overview over the interview quotes supporting these conclusions is presented.  
Besides the findings for each previously stated assumption, some general findings from the interviews 
can also be introduced and discussed. These, for example, refer to the type of use-case the interview 
==PAGE:64==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 56 
 
participants typically work with when integrating collaborative robot systems. These observations 
allow to either confirm or challenge the selection of use-cases that was made in Chapter 5.1.1.  
Here, some general themes can be observed. Handling seems to be a strong focus with different use-
cases in this group having a similar focus: 
• 
Handling use-cases in general (Interview 8, [00:13:30]; Interview 12, [00:18:59]) 
• 
Item/pin picking (Interview 5, [00:00:14]; Interview 10, [00:13:35]; Interview 12, [00:18:59]) 
• 
Palletising (Interview 1, [00:19:34]; Interview 5, [00:00:14]; Interview 7, [00:07:14]; Interview 
10 [00:13:35]) 
• 
Machine Tending (Interview 3, [00:03:14]; Interview 4, [00:18:42]; Interview 9, [00:22:14]; 
Interview 11, [00:08:24]) 
• 
Packing (Interview 10, [00:13:35])  
Further, the group of joining and dispensing is mentioned with welding use-cases being the focus area 
of four participants (Interview 2, [00:01:25]; Interview 6, [00:01:10]; Interview 9, [00:22:14]; Interview 
10, [00:13:35]). An outlier from the group of finishing is named by one participant: sanding (Interview 
12, [00:18:59]). Neither assembly nor quality inspection use-cases receive specific focus from the 
interviewees. The main areas are different handling use-cases and welding applications.  
Comparing these results to the previously introduced groups (compare Chapter 5.1.1), a positive 
finding is that no use-cases were mentioned in the interviews that cannot be classified within one of 
the five groups. However, two of the five groups were not specifically mentioned in the interviews. 
Therefore, a consideration can be to limit the selection of use-case groups further since only some of 
the groups receive the majority of attention from system integrators. This conclusion would be based 
on a limited dataset. The focus on some use-cases should be kept in mind for the following 
elaborations to set the interview quotes into context.  
5.2.2.1 
Underlying: There is an actual need for truly collaborative robot system 
Questions about the underlying assumption were not always explicitly covered in the interviews. This 
is due to the time constraint of 30 minutes that was set for the interviews. The focus for this limited 
timeframe was set on gathering data on the following three assumptions. Still, relevant quotes and 
thoughts for the thematic analysis of whether there is a need for collaborative robot systems could be 
combined from those participants that were explicitly asked combined with those that implicitly 
added valuable considerations.  
Only one participant disregarded the idea of physical collaboration stating, “I think in more than 95% 
of the industrial use for these cobots, you need to fence it in” (Interview 2, [00:11:14]). This statement 
should be considered in the context that he mostly works with welding applications, which are 
typically viewed as being more hazardous. Nonetheless, the participant did not see a use-case in an 
industrial setting that would be solvable without physical separation (Interview 2, [00:11:29]). Another  
==PAGE:65==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 57 
 
participant shared this assessment, though in a less strict way. He only observed that some use-cases 
are not applicable to be solved with physical separation, with the main deciding factor for this from 
his perspective being high payload of the robot since they would make complying with the norms 
infeasible (Interview 5, [00:11:18]). This limitation does not change his view on collaborative robotics 
in general. Him saying, “so my opinion on fencing collaborative robots. You shouldn't do it, then [...] 
you shouldn't use a collaborative robot.” (Interview 5, [00:20:39]) clearly shows that from his 
perspective there is a need for use-cases in which robot and human work without physical separation.  
This view is explicitly shared by two other interview participants. While not dismissing the need for 
physical separation in certain cases, one participant sees the possibility for use-cases with physical 
collaboration since “it really depends on the application and there are very many applications, [that] 
wouldn't need to be fenced in” (Interview 8, [00:16:27]). From his perspective, this is justified since the 
risks or hazards present in a given (production) system do not increase and are still acceptable when 
adding a collaborative robot (Interview 8, [00:17:16]). Extending this view, another participant stated 
that “I really like the idea to have a robot collaborative together with people” (Interview 11, [00:17:10]) 
also stating, like participant 5, that you should look for other robot brands if you are using it with a 
fence. For the use-cases that participant 11 works with the possibility for human and robot to 
collaborate can be justified by the low speeds that the robot moves with (Interview 11, [00:17:36]). 
In those cases where safety is not ensured with physical separation, for example described by 
participants 5, 8, and 11, one still needs to consider the definition of collaboration, specifically the 
levels of collaboration, to be able to answer the formulated assumption. This is brought up by two 
participants. Participant 1 stated, “I think up until now we didn't reach the highest collaborative [...] 
interference” (Interview 1, [00:00:16]), and participant 12 added, “only 10% of our installations are 
collaborative [...] maybe 5 to 10%. The other ones are [...] more that they are in a coexistence” 
(Interview 12, [00:07:12]). Even though the focus is on lower levels of collaboration as also observed 
in the previous analysis (compare Chapter 5.1.1), these lower levels of collaboration still require 
specific consideration for collaborative operational modes. Therefore, the demand for collaborative 
robot systems can still be observed with this limitation in mind.  
In addition to the capabilities for physical collaboration, no matter the level of collaboration, some 
interview participants also noted other reasons for adapting collaborative robot systems. These can 
further show the need for such systems. On the one hand, the potential flexibility of a collaborative 
robot system is mentioned as a selling argument (Interview 3, [00:24:56]). This is based on the 
potential of easier programming of such a robot, which is also mentioned by participant 5 as an 
argument for choosing a collaborative robot even though physical separation might be needed to 
assure safe operation (Interview 5, [00:20:39]). This could also allow human operators who are 
unfamiliar with automation but who are familiar with the process that is to be automated to program 
the robot system. This might be needed for processing use-cases in which knowledge about the 
==PAGE:66==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 58 
 
conducted task is vital for reaching the desired result. This knowledge has to be transferred from the 
human process specialist to the robot systems, which is made possible with collaborative robot 
systems. The argument is presented by participant 10 (Interview 10, [00:19:48]). Therefore, the ease 
of programming could be a valid reason for end customers to choose a collaborative robot system. 
The argument does however not fully apply to system integrators (Interview 5, [00:20:39]). 
With this, it can be concluded that the underlying assumption for this work is not confirmed. While 
only individual participants disregarded the need for physical collaboration completely, most industry 
professionals could at least see some use for applications in which human and robot share a 
workspace. However, the collaboration is mostly restricted to lower levels of collaboration. In 
addition, other potential benefits of collaborative robot systems (flexibility, ease of programming) that 
have been previously described in this work (compare Chapter 1) are observed as other, sometimes 
stronger factors in the adaptation of collaborative robot systems in industrial practice. While 
collaborative systems are in strong demand for a variety of reasons, broader adoption of higher 
collaboration levels is unlikely. Nonetheless, the impetus for this work is not disputed. While higher 
levels of collaboration would certainly require a more extensive risk assessment, the lower levels of 
collaboration already place high demands on the personnel tasked with defining the safety aspects of 
an application. As stated, for example, by participant 11: “you still have to make the risk assessment” 
(Interview 11, [00:18:02]). With this, the aim of further exploring the landscape of use-cases for 
collaborative robot systems has been achieved. Furthermore, the general need for collaborative robot 
systems could be confirmed, just not towards the higher levels of collaboration. Therefore, a more 
systematic approach for the hazard analysis that offers more support to the users might still be needed 
and required. Further exploration of this question is presented in the next subchapter.  
5.2.2.2 
A1: People need support for the hazard analysis for collaborative robot systems 
The assumption was made that the analysis of hazards is more complex for collaborative robot 
systems and that therefore more support is needed. First, the risk assessment is always required: “if 
it's intended as a collaborative robot [...] the risk assessment is [...] a necessity, but [if] it's not in a 
collaborative application, still risk assessment is a necessity, but because of other means [...] in a 
different approach” (Interview 10, [00:08:09]). These different approaches highlight one aspect of the 
complexity. The complexity can also be observed with participants stating that experience is needed 
to conduct a risk assessment, which is mentioned by five interview participants (Interview 2, 
[00:06:18]; Interview 4, [00:24:57]; Interview 6, [00:04:55]; Interview 7, [00:01:53]; Interview 12, 
[00:19:40]). Especially the question of which risks to consider in the mitigation and which risk to leave 
as residual risks is a challenge noted, for example, by participant 9. This question is difficult to answer 
since existing definitions for the term risk would be vague (Interview 9, [00:05:33]). 
People then deploy different strategies to use their experience or combat the issues they face with 
missing experience. For some, the issue is resolved by having a set of standard applications in which 
==PAGE:67==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 59 
 
they can more easily gain experience (Interview 3, [00:12:02]; Interview 4, [00:14:53]; Interview 6 
[00:09,08]). Equivalently, participant 1 mentions previous risk assessments as a data source for risk 
identification and estimation (Interview 1, [00:08:26]). This already shows that there must be 
similarities between different use-cases which will be explored in the next subchapter. Others use 
their experience to ask the system designers easy questions that help them understand safety aspects 
(Would you dare to put your finger here if I turn the system on? If no, you need to fence it in; Would 
you feel good having your kids work with this machine? (Interview 4, [00:24:57])). Similarly, participant 
1 uses a finger made of rubber to put it into certain hazardous areas of the machine to assess the 
potential for injury (Interview 1, [00:09:11]). 
The assistance of external expert companies is mentioned by one participant as a strategy to combat 
their lacking experience (Interview 3, [00:09:34]). Another strategy is “to work in a very structured way 
[because] then it's [...] nice to do this work” (Interview 12, [00:19:40]). This structured risk assessment 
process is often guided and assisted by the use of certain tools. Spreadsheet templates and checklists 
are mentioned by four participants (Interview 2, [00:02:45]; Interview 4, [00:02:13]; Interview 5, 
[00:01:46]; Interview 8, [00:01:34]). Others use specific software tools for risk assessment (Interview 
1, [00:00:16]; Interview 3, [00:10:44]; Interview 12, [00:01:30]). These tools can either help guide the 
user through the process (CEDOC [101], participant 12; Safexpert [102], participant 1) or help in the 
modelling of safety-related control components (SISTEMA [103], participant 3). Besides software 
tools, assessment systematics like the fault tree analysis are mentioned (Interview 7, [00:01:53]). 
What is important from the participants´ answers is the iterative nature of the structured risk 
assessment. Often, a first iteration is done at the early stages of designing a collaborative robot 
application. This could, for example, be during the offer stage/concept study of a system so that 
safety-related information can be presented to the potential customer (Interview 6, [00:04:55]; 
Interview 7, [00:01:53]; Interview 12, [00:01:30]). A more detailed iteration would then have to follow 
once the offer is placed, and the system is being designed. Hereby, the risk assessment is performed 
in the early stages by looking at digital designs and 3D drawings (Interview 2, [00:08:35]; Interview 3, 
[00:08:56]; Interview 12, [00:01:30]). Typical questions in the initial phase are about the process, tool, 
workpiece, robot speed, payload, and the environment (Interview 6, [00:04:55], Interview 12, 
[00:01:30]). To have more detailed considerations for the environment, one participant also added 
that the risk assessment is performed at the customer site (Interview 7, [00:01:53]) since “you will 
always miss certain details” (Interview 7, [00:20:59]) otherwise.  
After the design stage, when the system has been constructed, some participants note a further 
iteration to assess “if there are some things, which we did not see in the first time” (Interview 3, 
[00:08:17]). With the completed machine, the iteration can contain force and pressure measurements 
that can validate that the collaborative system keeps within the limit values given in ISO/TS 15066. 
These measurements are used to assess the need of adding additional safety features (Interview 3, 
==PAGE:68==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 60 
 
[00:06:06]). Force and pressure measurements are also noted by participant 1 as the biggest 
difference to other industrial robots (Interview 1, [00:00:16]) and by participant 8 as a particular 
challenge (Interview 8, [00:01:11]). In response to the challenge of force and pressure measurements, 
participant 8 states: “We are of the opinion that in about 90% of the cases the pure mathematical 
consideration is sufficient” (Interview 8, [00:15:37]) instead of needing to conduct a series of extensive 
force and pressure measurements.  
Similarly, one participant only uses the standard “as background knowledge” (Interview 5, [00:01:46]) 
since “the 15066 is impossible to work with for our engineers” (Interview 5, [00:07:56]). Some 
statements would contradict themselves. Therefore, they derived rules of thumb and their own 
mathematical models for specific situations that are used in daily work. These rules still comply with 
the standards (Interview 5, [00:07:56]). One particular challenge in complying with the standards in 
these rules of thumb is that the occurrence of contact between robot system and human operator is 
only permitted at very low speeds in certain body areas the human (e.g., the kneecap). Avoiding these 
areas is therefore a key design restriction. (Interview 5, [00:06:20]). In other areas contact between 
human and robot is not permitted at all (contact with the head and neck area). This restriction, if 
applied strictly, would “stop any setup from being used without fences” (Interview 5, [00:25:22]). In 
the participant's experience, the certification of collaborative robot systems is more challenging when 
trying to strictly apply the standards (Interview 5, [00:25:22]).  
In addition to the force and pressure measurements, the standards are mentioned by one participant 
as a challenge when integrating a collaborative robot system into an existing system. The standards, 
which pursue a high level of safety, would lead to other parts of the system that were previously 
defined as being safe to work with now needing to comply with more strict regulations. (Interview 5, 
[00:21:44]). Furthermore, the standards would be unclear as to when additional safety measures are 
needed or when the collaborative operational modes can be safely applied (Interview 2, [00:04:49]; 
Interview 9, [00:12:32]). This has to do with vague definitions (Interview 9, [00:16:35]). Generally, the 
choice of suitable mitigation strategies seems to be a challenge (Interview 11, [00:00:13]).  
On the contrary, participant 4 mentioned a different standard (ISO 12100) on a positive note and as a 
tool that he uses to identify risks and hazards by going through the lists which are part of the standard 
(Interview 4, [00:04:41]). These are, according to him, only a starting point for the risk assessment 
since “it doesn't offer you any guidance on how to engineer” (Interview 4, [00:07:01]) and it does not 
present specific enough information about risk reduction which extends the previous point that the 
choice of mitigation strategy is seen as a challenge. ISO 12100 is also specifically mentioned as the 
basis for risk assessment by four other participants (Interview 1, [00:05:31]; Interview 2, [00:02:49]; 
Interview 8, [00:01:11]; Interview 9, [00:04:22]). Even though the standard was only mentioned in five 
interviews, it is not possible to conclude that other interview participants do not use the standards 
==PAGE:69==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 61 
 
just because they have not specifically mentioned them. In some cases, the questions provoking 
answers with regard to the standard have been assessed to be leading by the interviewer.  
Besides the discussion on standardisation, different strategies for identifying risks and hazards have 
been mentioned. Some try and go through the process the robot system is tasked with to identify risks 
at the different process steps (Interview 6, [00:07:26]). Others rather go through the different system 
parts, where the tool receives a majority of the attention (Interview 8, [00:05:20]). And again, others 
do both (Interview 7, [00:11:35]). In this identification, there are also specific considerations for 
human errors instead of just looking at the technical actors of the system (Interview 9, [00:08:59]). 
These different strategies for identifying hazards will be further studied in the next subchapter. For 
now only the finding should be noted that different ways to try and conduct a risk assessment exist.  
In the identification of hazards through either following the process or the different parts of the robot 
system, the description of the intended use of the machine is described as a challenge. The different 
situations for interaction between human and robot are vital for identifying hazardous situations 
(Interview 12, 00:07:12). Describing foreseeable situations in which human and robot interact can also 
assist in identifying potential misuse of the system which can, as described before, be a core 
consideration in the hazard identification.  
With these challenges the need to learn more about the risk assessment process is recognised by the 
interviewed experts. This is done through training (Interview 9, [00:01:46]) which, however, offers 
limited specific guidance for collaborative robot systems. Therefore, more information must be 
gathered which can be through the study of standards (e.g., ISO/TS 15066) (Interview 9, [00:04:44]). 
These standards are not always sufficient (Interview 9, [00:28:37]) as explored previously.  
To answer the first assumption, which stated that the risk assessment is more complex for 
collaborative systems and that therefore more support would be needed, one can conclude that the 
added complexity in collaborative systems can be confirmed when analysing the conducted 
interviews. The challenges are also not sufficiently met by the existing standards, so more support is 
likely needed. This is also explicitly stated by two interview participants: “you try to improve the 
process or describe it [...] in a systematic way [...] I would make good use of it” (Interview 1, [00:25:59]) 
and “I think there is a [...] lot [...] more people than actually think they should read it that should read 
it” (Interview 12, [00:22:39]). This can also be explained by a difference in perception. The marketing 
message for collaborative systems focuses on easy entry while there are certain challenges, for 
example regarding CE certification and risk assessment, that the customer of a collaborative system 
in the following needs to learn about (Interview 10, [00:03:41]). An additional aspect of this issue is 
that currently, some companies are completely unaware of safety-related aspects of their systems 
and therefore do not consider it from the start (Interview 11, [00:18:26]). These companies can also 
likely not rely on previous experiences, which is a strategy applied by the experts interviewed for this 
work, so again the need for additional guidance and more support is highlighted.  
==PAGE:70==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 62 
 
Therefore, in conclusion, assumption A1 can be confirmed. The hazard analysis, and in that especially 
the hazard mitigation through for example PFL, is more challenging for collaborative robot systems. 
Existing standards do not have a sufficient answer for these challenges. In addition, there is a need for 
inexperienced collaborative system users to learn more about safety aspects and to receive more 
support. A variety of options to conduct a risk assessment hereby complicate the search for additional 
guidance. This confirms the need for the systematic process which is developed in this work. The 
observed iterative nature of the risk assessment process which is followed by industry experts also 
adds to the systematic process, since the idea for the systematic is to give guidance to a designer of a 
collaborative robot system in the early stages of the design process while a more detailed risk 
assessment would have to follow in the later stages of designing the system.  
5.2.2.3 
A2: There are typical hazards and typical mitigation strategies in collaborative systems 
As mentioned, the next chapter aims to analyse whether there are hazards that are typically present 
in a collaborative robot system and if there are mitigation strategies that are typically applied to 
reduce the risk related to those hazards. In addition, if these typical hazards and mitigation strategies 
exist, they should in the following be identified and described.  
Generally, the opinion is stated that “every machine is different” (Interview 2, [00:01:51]), that certain 
factors have to be assessed “case by case” (Interview 11, [00:05:48]), and that every machine has to 
be re-evaluated in special machine construction (Interview 4, [00:24:57]; Interview 7, [00:07:14]; 
Interview 8, [00:00:13]). However, also in those cases where the risk assessment must be done for 
each machine, the first considerations regarding factors that influence the safety concept of a system 
are always similar. The speed, weight/payload, force/clamping force and the tool of the robot are 
always core factors (Interview 4, [00:22:36]; Interview 6, [00:04:55]; Interview 7, [00:01:53]; Interview 
10, [00:09:42]; Interview 11, [00:02:51]). This also holds for the ensuing risk assessment, where one 
expert states: “I've been doing these risk assessments for close to 15 years and not very much has 
changed” (Interview 4, [00:02:13]).  
Adding to this, the opinion is also stated that the risks “in most cases [...] are very similar” (Interview 
2, [00:01:51]). This is especially stated by those experts that try and work with a set of standard 
applications that they can offer to their customers (Interview 3, [00:14:35]; Interview 4, [00:08:10]; 
Interview 5, [00:10:06]; Interview 6, [00:09:08]; Interview 12, [00:12:49]). For example, “simple” 
handling tasks are mentioned as a case where the hazards are similar (Interview 5, [00:16:16]). The 
limitation to a narrower set of use-cases allows for a simplified analysis (Interview 12, [00:11:12]). 
Both the factors that are considered for assessing safety-relevant decisions in special built machines, 
as well as the hazards that are similar in standard applications, will be more thoroughly discussed in 
this chapter. What can already be concluded with this, however, is that there are typical hazards that 
must be considered in collaborative systems, confirming the first part of the second assumption. What 
requires further study is whether the tool and the workpiece are the most relevant hazard sources.  
==PAGE:71==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 63 
 
Similar comments, even if not stated as explicitly, can be observed for the hazard mitigation of 
collaborative robot systems. One participant said that the considerations for mitigation are similar for 
all systems and even comparable to typical industrial robots (Interview 3, [00:15:36]). Another 
participant explained that he applies the same safety strategy for all systems (Interview 6, [00:18:42]). 
Participant 8 also always follows certain mitigation strategies depending on the severity of injury 
(Interview 8, [00:12:30]). Finally, participant 12 uses a strategy in which certain design constraints are 
always in place that work towards reducing common risks (Interview 12, [00:12:49]). In the 
elaborations of other interview participants, certain safety functions or safety features that are 
typically used to make collaborative robot systems safer to work with can also be observed. These 
must first be studied more deeply before a full conclusion on the stated assumption is possible. This 
will be done after a detailed analysis of the typical hazards. 
Typical Hazards  
As stated, the assumption that typical hazards exist in collaborative systems has been confirmed. The 
following aims to further analyse these hazards that have been described by the participating experts. 
The first clarification to this end is that the hazard categories or types are not the most important 
consideration. While there are similarities in the hazard types that can be observed between different 
use-cases, the hazard sources are the more relevant consideration.  
The first consideration in the discussion of hazard sources is the robot arm itself as well as movements 
from the arm (Interview 4, [00:19:35]; Interview 5, [00:03:31]; Interview 7, [00:09:36]; Interview 12, 
[00:01:30]). In the consideration for the robot arm, the speed of the robot is, as discussed earlier, a 
core factor. This is mentioned by six participants in the context of hazard identification (Interview 2, 
[00:11:29]; Interview 3, [00:17:38]; Interview 4, [00:09:12]; Interview 7, [00:03:44]; Interview 10, 
[00:09:42]; Interview 11, [00:02:51]). In other interviews, the factor speed is also mentioned as a 
possible risk mitigation strategy, which will be introduced when discussing typical mitigation.  
For machine tending applications, the speed is discussed contrastingly. One participant describes the 
robot speeds as too high for collaborative operation due to the demanded cycle time (Interview 3, 
[00:17:38]). Another participant argues for lower robot speeds since the machining times in the CNC 
machine would anyways greatly outweigh the time needed for the handling task of the robot. 
Therefore, the desired cycle time would not be influenced by the robot movements (Interview 11, 
[00:05:48]). Here, differences between individual use-cases can be observed which shows that every 
machine must undergo a complete risk assessment in which certain factors are assessed.   
With the considerations for the speed of the robot, one can also add the process the robot follows/is 
tasked with as a source of hazards. Participant 6 and participant 12 consider this in their analysis 
(Interview 6, [00:07:26]; Interview 12, [00:01:30]). Participant 9 does not only consider the process 
but also the software program of the robot (Interview 9, [00:19:28]). Errors in the program as a source 
of hazards are also mentioned by two other participants (Interview 7, [00:14:28]; Interview 12, 
==PAGE:72==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 64 
 
[00;14:30]). If the process is comparable between different systems, for example if different welding 
applications are designed as it is the case for participant 3, the mechanical design of the system 
receives more attention (Interview 2, [00:08:05]). The mechanical design, aside from the robot arm 
itself, depends on the tool that is attached to the end of arm of the robot. For example, participant 1 
states “mostly it's the tool of the robot” (Interview 1, [00:16:53]). The end of arm tool as an important 
source of hazards is mentioned by five other participants (Interview 4, [00:22:10]; Interview 5, 
[00:03:31]; Interview 7, [00:19:46]; Interview 8, [00:05:41]; Interview 11, [00:02:51]). The 
consideration for this source obviously depends on the particular end effector. A vacuum gripper is 
typically assessed to present fewer hazards. Three times use-cases using a such a gripper are discussed 
as being simpler from a safety point of view. This is due to the low weight and the removed clamping 
risk of these tools compared to other handling solutions. (Interview 5, [00:17:32]; Interview 7, 
[00:19:46]; Interview 9, [00:12:32]). In addition to the tool, the workpiece that is handled or processed 
in the robot system is a core hazard source. This is mentioned by five experts (Interview 3, [00:19:01]; 
Interview 5, [00:03:31]; Interview 8, [00:05:41]; Interview 11, [00:02:51]; Interview 12, [00:01:30]). All 
experts that have named the tool or the workpiece as a hazard source work with handling applications.  
Summarising, as participant 1 stated, “everything that's moving” (Interview 1, [00:16:53) has to be 
considered in the first step of the hazard identification. This includes the robot system itself with 
attached tools as well as peripherals and the environment. The environment, however, is something 
that is overlooked by some. As participant 5 stated when talking about working together with his 
colleagues: “typically they thought a lot about the robots and the tool it's holding [...] but actually often 
[...] the hazards overlooked are in the environment” (Interview 5, [00:03:31]). The interviewed experts 
do, however, place specific considerations on identifying hazards in the environment. This could be 
related to attached machines or peripherals, but also with regard to the layout of the system. In total, 
explicit mentions for the environment or the layout of the collaborative system can be found in seven 
interviews (Interview 1, [00:16:53]; Interview 3, [00:24:22]; Interview 5, [00:18:28]; Interview 7, 
[00:19:46]; Interview 10, [00:09:42]; Interview 11, [00:14:43]; Interview 12, [00:12:49]). Participant 7 
even goes one step further, mentioning that an iteration of the risk assessment is performed on the 
site of integration to ensure that no hazards are overlooked (Interview 7, [00:18:33]). In most cases 
when the layout of the system is mentioned for the identification it is perceived as an added challenge. 
Either because it can not be changed and it is a constraint that is given for the design process 
determined by the shopfloor layout of the customer (Interview 3, [00:24:22]; Interview 7, [00:23:01]; 
Interview 11, [00:14:43]; Interview 12, [00:12:49]) or because the layout is determined with regard to 
the performance of the system (Interview 5, [00:18:28]). For the layout, the possibility of a human 
operator to access hazardous areas is a question that must be considered in the risk assessment 
(Interview 5, [00:13:33]; Interview 7, [00:23:01]; Interview 9, [00:02:31]; Interview 12, [00:15:22]). 
Hereby again, surrounding machines next to the robot are an important factor. For example, as stated 
by participant 5: “typically our robot is standing next to a conveyor [...] and often the edges [...] are 
==PAGE:73==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 65 
 
simply [...] too sharp or not curved enough” (Interview 5, [00:04:31]). Similarly, participant 4 mentions 
CNC machines that are placed next to the robot in a machine tending application. The machine is 
opened without physical barriers, meaning a human could reach into the machine while parts like the 
clamp of the CNC machine could move (Interview 4, [00:19:35]). Another example would be a 
turntable that is added to a welding system (Interview 6, [00:09:52]). This again highlights possible 
hazards in the environment of the robot system where the attached machines can present hazards, 
even if they had been marked as being inherently safe to use with a CE-marking before the integration 
of a collaborative robot system. Everything in the vicinity of the robot must follow the rules 
accordingly, which can be challenging since production lines previously considered safe must now 
comply with higher standards (Interview 5, [00:21:44]). 
In all these considerations for different hazard sources, both relating to the collaborative robot system 
itself, but also the workpiece or the environment, there is one predominant hazard category. As stated 
in one interview: “it's mostly mechanical hazards” (Interview 1, [00:13:10]) Mechanical hazards and 
contact situations are explicitly mentioned by five participants as a main hazard (Interview 1, 
[00:13:10]; Interview 4, [00:22:10]; Interview 5, [00:16:16]; Interview 8, [00:05:41]; Interview 12, 
[00:14:30]). This could be related to pinching, squeezing, or any other contact situation between 
human and robot. For this, sharp edges of the different hazard sources receive special considerations 
since these increase the potential injury severity of the mechanical hazard. Six interviews have 
mentions of sharp edges (Interview 3, [00:17:38]; Interview 5, [00:03:31]; Interview 6, [00:08:05]; 
Interview 7, [00:06:11]; Interview 8, [00:10:34]; Interview 11, [00:12:21]).  
Besides mechanical hazards, electrical hazards are discussed by the experts. Some state that they will 
always have some electrical hazards, for example, related to safe signal processing or the switch 
cabinet (Interview 1, [00:13:10]; Interview 8, 00:06:04). Others rather exclaim that the presence of 
electrical hazards depends on the application and that they are not always present. The same would 
then hold for pneumatical hazards which could be related to the gripper for example (Interview 4, 
[00:21:48]; Interview 5, [00:16:16]; Interview 12, [00:11:12]). 
Application-specific hazards can particularly be observed for welding applications. Specifically, these 
would be thermal and radiation/UV lighting hazards that stem from the welding tool at the end of arm 
of the robot (Interview 2, [00:06:54]; Interview 6, [00:12:11]; Interview 7, [00:07:14]; Interview 9, 
[00:22:57]). Also relevant for welding applications are the mechanical hazards that can be traced back 
to the welding end effector where the sharp welding wire is placed that can act as a needle (Interview 
2, [00:06:54]; Interview 9, [00:12:32]).  
Other applications also receive some specific considerations. Handling applications are regarded as 
easier by some (Interview 1, [00:19:34]). They can, however, also present additional challenges. For 
example, in a situation where human and robot work at the same height so that contact between the 
head of the operator and the robot could occur (Interview 5, [00:13:33]). In a further handling 
==PAGE:74==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 66 
 
application, namely palletising, the layout of the system is an important consideration since the 
placement of the pallet and other obstacles in the environment influence the occurrence of hazards 
greatly (Interview 5, [00:13:33]; Interview 7, [00:18:33]). Here, robot movements in the space 
accessible to the human (which typically are movements outside of the pallet area) are relevant.  
Another consideration could be human error where the human operator places a body part in an area 
that was deemed inaccessible to the human, for example on top of the pallet (Interview 7, [00:09:36]). 
Generally, human error is a hazard source that is rarely considered explicitly but that is still important. 
Besides the description for a palletising use-case by participant 7, it is only mentioned by participant 
9 who puts “very much weight on the unintended use of the machine” (Interview 9, [00:10:01]), and 
participant 12 who also tries to identify foreseeable misuses of the machine (Interview 12, [00:09:14]). 
That only a minority of participants has explicit considerations for these types of hazards is in line with 
the previous finding that the perspective on safety aspects is techno-centric and that it should be 
extended to include considerations for all actors of the system.  
Similarly, a hazard source that has been noted by some experts but that should receive more attention 
in the identification is the unforeseeable behaviour of a robot system. As discussed previously, human 
awareness is a core concept in collaborative systems. This has also been recognised by participant 9 
who notes the unintended behaviour of the robot as a source of hazards (Interview 9, [00:18:57]). 
Participant 6 has even more extensive consideration where the entire safety strategy is built around 
creating predictable behaviour of the robot system and communicating intent between robot and 
human (Interview 6, [00:12:11]). 
Summarising, hazard sources that must typically be evaluated in collaborative robot systems exist. 
Those can relate to the robot system itself. Within this, the robot arm, the robot speed, the 
programming/process that is followed by the robot, and the tool that is attached to the end of arm 
are important factors. Furthermore, the workpiece or object which is handled or processed by the 
system can present hazards. The environment in which the robot system is placed can add additional 
hazards related to attached machinery, environmental conditions or the layout where access 
possibilities to hazard sources must be considered.  
A narrower group of experts also considers human error and human awareness as sources of hazards. 
While they have not been discussed in depth during most interviews, they should not be disregarded. 
With the findings from the previous theoretical research in mind, some weight should be placed on 
these factors for the continued discussions. All hazard sources mostly present mechanical hazards. 
Other hazard categories should however also be part of a thorough risk assessment. Particularly 
welding use-cases necessitate considerations for thermal and radiation hazards.  
Hereby, the second assumption is further confirmed. The tool and the workpiece constitute relevant 
hazard sources. However, limiting the analysis to these sources would not be sufficient as explored in 
this chapter. Therefore, the assumption cannot be fully confirmed without noting other relevant 
==PAGE:75==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 67 
 
hazard sources. With these findings related to typical hazards in collaborative systems, the next part 
will analyse mitigation strategies that are typically used to reduce the risks related to these hazards.  
Typical Mitigation Strategies 
Mitigating hazards in a collaborative robot system is not only about reducing the potential of injury. 
While this is the main aim, it is also about creating a comfortable work environment for human 
operators. Especially those that are unfamiliar with automation need to be taken into consideration 
so that the chosen mitigation strategy gives them a safe feeling, which might include adding more 
safety features than necessary (Interview 6, [00:16:50]; Interview 7, [00:04:32]). To achieve this, the 
three steps in which risks can be reduced are first constructive measures, second technical measures, 
and third organisational measures (Interview 8, [00:08:51]). The typical mitigation strategies discussed 
by the expert participants are in the following introduced in this order.  
Constructive measures in the first place include considerations to have round edges and remove sharp 
corners from the collaborative workspace. This is explicitly used by four participants to reduce risks 
(Interview 5, [00:03:31]; Interview 6, [00:08:05]; Interview 7, [00:06:11]; Interview 8, [00:10:34]). 
Another possibility, especially if sharp edges cannot be changed, is to limit the possibility of accessing 
these hazard sources. Encapsulating specific hazardous areas, for example a welding wire, while 
keeping the availability of physical collaboration in the rest of the workspace is mentioned by two 
participants (Interview 2, [00:06:54]; Interview 8, [00:10:34]). Besides encapsulating specific areas, the 
layout of the collaborative robot system is something that receives considerations from the system 
integrators. For example, placing the robot system strategically to use given constraints like walls as 
an option to remove access to the system is mentioned (Interview 7, [00:03:44]; Interview 11, 
[00:15:21]). Another possibility would be to design the workspace in a way that reduces harmful 
spaces for the operators (Interview 12, [00:15:22]). What is also mentioned to this end are limits to 
the robot's range of movement, for example, limiting the joint angles or adding safety planes that the 
robot cannot move through. This is discussed in three interviews (Interview 3, [00:23:31]; Interview 7, 
[00:06:11]; Interview 8, [00:15:06]). The safety planes could however also be classified as a technical 
reduction instead of a constructive measure (Interview 8, [00:12:30]). 
When designing the work scene other important considerations are “how you move the robot [and] 
how you hold in products” (Interview 12, [00:15:22]). This is to limit the risk of throwing away a 
workpiece and creating hazards with flying parts or to reduce a potential stabbing/cutting risk 
(Interview 8, [00:12:30]). Similarly, participant 4 notes the movement of the tool as a possible 
mitigation strategy. To remove the mechanical hazard of a body part being clamped by the gripping 
tool you could “make travel of your clamp smaller than five millimetres” (Interview 4, [00:22:36]). 
Generally, participant 4 only sees two options for risk reduction in collaborative applications: “it's just 
[...] limiting the travel or limiting the force” (Interview 4, [00:24:21]). Limiting the robots force is 
mentioned by seven additional participants (Interview 1, [00:00:16]; Interview 3, [00:06:06]; Interview 
==PAGE:76==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 68 
 
5 [00:10:06]; Interview 6, [00:08:05]; Interview 8, [00:01:53]; Interview 9, [00:08:59]; Interview 12, 
[00:07:12]). This is a potential technical risk reduction measure. Typically, the robot force can be 
reduced by limiting the speed at which the robot moves. Speed reduction is further, often in the same 
context as force reduction, mentioned by eight participants (Interview 1, [00:23:40]; Interview 3, 
[00:22:13]; Interview 5, [00:13:33]; Interview 6, [00:08:05]; Interview 8, [00:10:34]; Interview 9, 
[00:11:56]; Interview 11, [00:05:48]; Interview 12, [00:07:12]). With that, it can be observed that 
nearly all participants agree on limiting the robot's force and/or speed to mitigate hazards.  
Often, the speed is reduced in the operational mode SSM, so that the robot moves slower or 
completely stops when an operator gets closer. To achieve this, typically additional technical 
components are necessary. Three components are mentioned by the participants: laser range 
scanners, safety mats, and light curtains. Range scanners are mentioned in seven interviews (Interview 
1, [00:23:40]; Interview 3, [00:16:31]; Interview 5, [00:13:33]; Interview 7, [00:03:44]; Interview 8, 
[00:03:00]; Interview 9, [00:11:22]; Interview 11, [00:01:27]) and receive the largest share of 
attention. It is also stated twice that range scanners are used in almost all systems (Interview 7, 
[00:04:32]; Interview 9, [00:11:22]). Light curtains, mentioned four times (Interview 2, [00:03:45]; 
Interview 6, [00:09:52]; Interview 7, [00:06:11]; Interview 11, [00:02:06]), and safety mats, mentioned 
twice (Interview 5, [00:13:33]; Interview 7, [00:06:11]), receive less attention and can also be viewed 
as an alternative for the case that range scanners are not desired for a specific application (Interview 
7, [00:06:11]). If the technical measures are not sufficient to reduce risks, the system would need to 
be fenced in or separated from the operator (Interview 11, [00:14:07]). This could be for welding 
applications (Interview 2, [00:11:57]) or when a hazardous tool is used (Interview 7, [00:12:35]). 
A final technical measure that is mentioned in the interviews are enabling devices that are used to 
move the robot (Interview 3, [00:16:31]; Interview 4, [00:17:11]). These could also be used to ensure 
the safe operation of individual parts of the machine like a turntable (Interview 6, [00:09:52]) or to 
assure that a human operator can only enter the workspace when a dangerous tool like a welding 
torch is turned off (Interview 6, [00:16:50]). This technical measure could be seen as a form of 
communication between the human operator and robot system. This links to organisational measures, 
where the communication from the robot system to the operator is another possibility to reduce risks. 
This is mentioned by two participants where the information is conveyed through light and sound 
effects (Interview 6, [00:12:11]; Interview 9, [00:14:55]). While this measure has only been discussed 
in two interviews, the importance, especially considering the findings from previous theoretical 
research, should not be underestimated. The information about the system status and the operational 
mode must be conveyed to the operator to ensure that mode confusion does not lead to injury.  
An additional strategy to inform the user about how to use the system is the user manual. This 
document is a requirement for any CE-certified machine. Nonetheless, it is only specifically mentioned 
in three interviews (Interview 3, [00:09:01]; Interview 8, [00:10:34]; Interview 9, [00:24:43]). 
==PAGE:77==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 69 
 
Information presented in such a user manual could include information about PPE that must be worn 
when working with the system. This is another organisational measure, especially in those cases where 
the robot is integrated into an existing line where PPE was previously part of the risk reduction strategy 
(Interview 8, [00:08:51]). Furthermore, information with regard to the personnel that is allowed to 
use the machine could be presented. Specific operator training could in this way be another 
organisational measure to reduce risks when working with a machine (Interview 8, [00:08:51]).  
With all these possibilities to reduce risks, certain factors play into the decision of which measure to 
apply. Firstly, it is the application itself. For example, since it also presents unique hazards, unique 
mitigation strategies are used for welding applications. Welding torches can be water-cooled to 
reduce thermal hazards (Interview 7, [00:17:31]), windows or screens could be used to protect people 
walking past from UV lighting (Interview 6, [00:12:11]; Interview 9, [00:23:42]), and special care must 
be taken as to where to stop the robot, since stopping its movement in the middle of the weld would 
render that particular weld along with the workpiece useless (Interview 9, [00:14:55]).  
For other applications, different factors play a role in choosing suitable mitigation. Most obvious, the 
choice depends on how high the risk or potential for injury is (Interview 3, [00:19:01]; Interview 8, 
[00:12:30]; Interview 11, [00:04:47]). This can be traced back to the different risk factors like injury 
potential or chance of occurrence. Customer requirements or standards can also have an influence 
(Interview 1, [00:22:38]; Interview 2, [00:11:57]; Interview 3, [00:04:50]; Interview 5, [00:20:01]; 
Interview 8, [00:01:53]). Another factor can be the availability of parts (Interview 4, [00:12:23]). 
Summarising, the reduction of interaction forces through removing sharp edges and through reducing 
the robot speed is a mitigation strategy that can reduce risks in a collaborative robot system. Often, 
additional technical equipment such as laser range scanners is used. Furthermore, considerations for 
the layout of the system are important to remove the possibility of accessing hazardous areas. This 
consideration can be extended by using safety planes or joint limits to limit the movements of the 
robot. Further, the importance of ensuring high levels of communication between human and robot 
and between robot and human should not be underestimated. This can be, for example, through 
enabling devices or through using different channels for conveying information. Finally, 
complementary measures like PPE and operator training can be useful.  
With this, the conclusion can be drawn that also the second part of the stated assumption is correct. 
Strategies typically used to mitigate risks in collaborative robot systems exist. Therefore, assumption 
A2, that there are typical hazards and typical mitigation strategies in collaborative systems, is 
confirmed. These hazards and mitigation strategies have also been described as part of this 
subchapter. Especially with the description of typical hazards, one limitation in confirming the second 
assumption has to be made clear. The hazard sources are more diverse than just considerations for 
the tool of the robot and the workpiece that is handled. A complete and thorough risk assessment 
also necessitates studying, for example, the environment as well as human error and awareness.  
==PAGE:78==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 70 
 
5.2.2.4 
A3: Currently, in the analysis not all hazard sources are considered equally 
Part of the third assumption can already be disproved. It was stated that hazards in the broader work 
system receive little attention. In the discussion of typical hazards, it was revealed that the 
environment does receive attention in both the identification and even in the mitigation of hazards. 
In the identification, the environment, attached machines, etc. have been mentioned by more than 
half of the interview participants (Interview 1, Interview 3, Interview 5, Interview 7, Interview 10, 
Interview 11, Interview 12). Therefore, only the remaining part of the third assumption is left to be 
investigated in more detail. Specifically, that means that the following elaborations aim to find 
whether different phases of the life cycle of the collaborative robot system receive less attention.  
When asked about this, two participants explained that other life phases do receive specific 
considerations. Hazards are identified and the next step is to review in which phases of the machine 
life cycle, including maintenance, the specific hazard could occur (Interview 1, [00:15:07]; Interview 8, 
[00:07:19]). A result of this consideration could be that it is ensured that maintenance relevant parts 
are accessible (Interview 1, [00:17:49]). Another participant stated that, while different phases are 
considered, the mounting of the system receives more consideration and that maintenance 
operations are not part of the risk assessment (Interview 9, [00:20:46]). Similarly, a participant added 
that maintenance operations are theoretically part of the considerations but that in practice most 
effort is spent on the actual operation of the system. This is because maintenance is done by trained 
engineers and the occurrence of maintenance tasks is seldom (Interview 5, [00:12:31]).  
Adding to this, participants noted that they do not have specific considerations for maintenance 
operations for various reasons. One participant argues that the systems they are working with are not 
complex and that therefore maintenance tasks must not be considered specifically (Interview 3, 
[00:20:29]). Another participant stated that the system is anyways shut down during maintenance 
operations (Interview 2, [00:15:37]). For some, the consideration for maintenance is limited to noting 
in the user manual that the system must be switched off for maintenance operations like switching a 
tool (Interview 9, [00:20:46]). Change of tool is also noted by one further participant as the only 
considered maintenance tasks (Interview 1, [00:17:49]).  
The view on maintenance operations could, however, also be extended. Besides analysing it with 
regard to the identification of hazards, maintenance could also be part of a mitigation strategy. As 
stated by participant 7: “We have our own [...] cobot maintenance contracts [...] to make sure every 
year to just [...] get there, to see, okay: Is everything still working properly?” (Interview 7, [00:14:28]). 
While this view on maintenance was not intended with the stated assumption it is also mentioned by 
participant 11, who stated, that the customer has the responsibility for this preventive maintenance 
(Interview 11, [00:16:38]). Another reason, stated by one participant, for preventive maintenance not 
being specifically considered is a lack of experience (Interview 3, [00:19:36]).  
==PAGE:79==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 71 
 
Besides maintenance operation, when asked about different operational modes, considerations for 
an automatic mode and a reduced mode for hand guiding/teaching were described by six participants 
(Interview 2, [00:14:40]; Interview 3, [00:21:47]; Interview 4, [00:17:11]; Interview 5, [00:19:39]; 
Interview 6, [00:16:50]; Interview 9, [00:12:32]). The different modes are considered “because in 
automatic mode, everything is as safe as possible [...] and with a manual and teach you're using 
different safety modes” (Interview 4, [00:17:11]). These safety modes could, for example, be the 
previously mentioned enabling devices. Other considerations for teaching modes could also be that 
certain dangerous systems like a welding torch need to be switched off (Interview 6, [00:16:50]). 
Drawing the line between where reduced mode is enough and where additional measures need to be 
added is hereby stated as a particular challenge (Interview 9, [00:12:32]). 
A limitation of this analysis is that questions regarding maintenance operations and different 
operational modes could not always be part of the interview. This is due to time constraints and due 
to the focus on exploring the previous assumptions. Therefore, the conclusions drawn from this 
analysis should be considered carefully. When concluding the third assumption, it nonetheless has to 
be stated that not all phases of the machine have equal attention. Maintenance tasks rarely receive 
specific considerations in the identification of hazards. Therefore, part of the third assumption can be 
confirmed. As stated previously, the second part of the assumption concerning different sources has 
already been disproved. What can also be added as a finding is that the differentiation between an 
automatic mode and a reduced mode is a strategy deployed by the interview participants in the design 
of a collaborative robot system. This should be taken into consideration for the continued work.  
5.2.2.5 
Results 
With the analysis of the gathered interview data completed, the next chapter aims to give a brief 
overview of the results with regard to the stated assumptions. In addition, a summary of important 
findings of the analysis should be presented. First, each assumption will be disproven or confirmed 
with a comprehensive description as to why this conclusion can be drawn:  
• 
Underlying: There is an actual need for truly collaborative robot systems even though 
currently mainly co-existence is observed.  
Not confirmed: There is a need for collaborative robot systems in general for a variety of 
reasons, but not for higher levels of collaboration. Nonetheless, the topic of hazard analysis is 
still worthwhile to analyse in-depth.  
• 
A1: The risk assessment process is more complex from an analytical standpoint for 
collaborative systems. Therefore, people need support for the hazard analysis for 
collaborative robot systems on an analytical level. 
Confirmed: The hazard analysis, and in that especially the hazard mitigation through for 
example PFL, is more challenging for collaborative robot systems on an analytical level. 
Existing standards also do not have a sufficient answer for these challenges. In addition, there 
==PAGE:80==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 72 
 
is a need for inexperienced collaborative system users to learn more about safety aspects and 
to receive additional support. The iterative nature of the risk assessment for collaborative 
systems further supports the systematic process developed in this work.  
• 
A2: There are typical hazards and typical mitigation strategies in collaborative systems. Most 
often, the tool or workpiece are the most relevant hazard sources when only considering the 
robot system.  
Partially confirmed: Typical hazard sources as well as typical mitigation strategies exist. 
However, the hazard sources are more diverse than stated in the assumption. This must be 
taken into consideration. Sources can be the robot system including the tool, the workpiece, 
the environment, and human error. Mitigation for these hazards can be reducing interaction 
forces, adding technical mitigation like laser range scanners, removing access to hazardous 
areas, and creating a high level of communication between robot and human.  
• 
A3: Currently, in the analysis, not all hazard sources are considered equally. Some phases of 
the life cycle and hazards from the broader work system receive little attention. 
Partially confirmed: Participating experts, as discovered in the previous assumption, have 
specific considerations for the environment in which the robot system is placed. Therefore, 
part of the third assumption is disproven. However, maintenance operation and other phases 
of the machine's life seem to receive less attention leading to part of the assumption being 
confirmed. This conclusion, however, should be considered carefully since the topic was not 
covered in all interviews.  
To support these claims, the following Table 4 gives an overview of statements and key words with
