5.2.3 Adaptations to the Systematic Hazard Analysis  
The process presented in Chapter 5.1.2 lays the basis for the following discussions. To guide the system 
designer in the early stages of designing a collaborative robot application the process is based on a set 
of use-cases that are typical for collaborative robot systems. In the following, the designer is guided 
through different questions and considerations that should assist in evaluating if an operational mode 
is suited for the application that should be developed. Hereby, the main aim is to remove complexity 
from the detailed risk assessment that needs to follow the systematic process. To this end, different 
mitigation strategies that can be used in the collaborative operational modes are also described.  
The interview findings hereby confirm the main strategy behind the proposed systematic approach. 
First, it is confirmed that more support is needed especially to support inexperienced users. Second, 
typical hazards are described. Also for special built machines where the risk assessment has to be re-
evaluated for each machine. Third, the iterative nature of the risk assessment described by the 
industry experts aligns with the suggestion of the systematic process which must be followed by a 
more detailed iteration of the assessment. The suggested approach could hereby assist in the first 
evaluation to check if a collaborative operational mode can and should be chosen for the use-case. 
Industry professionals currently rely on their experience for this step of the design of a new system. 
The considered factors in this first assessment align with the described steps of the systematic process 
(speed, weight/payload, force/clamping force, and the tool of the robot).  
However, with the interview findings in mind, there are also certain improvements to the suggested 
systematic process. These can assist in making the approach more applicable to industrial use-cases. 
These changes should in the following be introduced and discussed. First, a narrower selection of use-
cases could improve the process. This will reduce the percentage of cases that are covered by the 
process. Hereby, the solution will be relevant to fewer users. However, the aim, as explained 
previously, is not to reach full coverage but rather to assist the development of the majority of use 
cases. Therefore, the use-cases in the suggested solution will be limited to handling use-cases, 
machine tending, palletising, and welding. Other use-cases could still benefit from the process which 
should be reflected. The more limited selection of included cases, however, will allow for more 
detailed information that can be presented for these application types. With that, a smaller number 
of users will receive more detailed assistance in the development of their use-cases.  
This improvement will be incorporated into the systematic process, which is still subject to the 
requirements as described in Chapter 5.1.2. Certain considerations are described for each use-case 
type that the system designer should be aware of when following the process. For handling, these 
==PAGE:84==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 76 
 
include the type of gripper, the handled object, and the combination of speed and weight. Machine 
tending extends the consideration to also assess risks related to attached machinery, the speed of the 
robot, and access requirements of human operators. Both for handling as well as for machine tending 
the consideration for sharp edges is relevant. In palletising, again the type of gripper and the (weight 
of the) handled items must be considered with care. In addition, obstacles in the environment can 
present new hazards. Furthermore, human error and misuse of the machine can create risks when a 
human operator is in the space above the pallet where the robot moves.  
Finally, welding applications, as described previously, have to be considered with the most care. 
Personal protective equipment and screens for personnel in the vicinity are vital in ensuring safe 
operation. Furthermore, cooling of the torch might be needed to allow collaboration. Finally, the 
stopping point of the robot must be considered with care. This is because stopping the robot´s 
movement in the mid of a weld would render that weld useless. A balance between process stability 
and operator safety must be achieved.  
With these considerations, the system designer will be guided through the process as described 
previously. The exit points to the process remain unchanged. One addition are more concrete 
considerations for the environment in which the collaborative system is placed. In the final step, after 
the process has been followed, a further change to the process is implemented. Namely, other, more 
useful information with regard to the different applicable hazard mitigation strategies in the three 
operational modes HG, PFL, and SSM is presented.  
HG is extended with considerations for predictable robot behaviour and consideration for different 
operational modes. Both PFL, as well as SSM, are extended with considerations for existing barriers in 
the environment and for safety planes that the robot system cannot move through. In addition, the 
aspect of removing the chance of a human error occurring is added as a factor that should be 
considered. The systematic process with the changes incorporated as described above is presented 
below in Figure 8. 
 
==PAGE:85==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Solution 
Page 77 
 
 
Figure 8: Changed Systematic Hazard Analysis for Collaborative Robot Systems (own figure) 
==PAGE:86==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Discussion 
Page 78 
 
6 Discussion  
This thesis builds towards a more systematic hazard analysis framework for the risk assessment of 
collaborative robot applications. The following section discusses the findings of this thesis by 
elaborating on the meaning, importance, and relevance of the results. This will be done first by 
describing the main findings and setting them into the context of the literature base. Second, the work 
will be critically reflected by arguing whether the goals presented in Chapter 3 have been achieved or 
not. In addition, the limitations of this work will be discussed. Third, this chapter will explore 
considerations that both industrial practitioners as well as researchers should take from this thesis for 
their future work.  
6.1 Analysis of Final Results 
This subchapter explains and evaluates the findings of this work and how they relate to the literature 
review. To this end, the most important findings are introduced. The thesis first concludes that 
industrial practitioners need more support for the hazard analysis of collaborative robot systems, 
which is a finding that is also supported by other researchers. Huck et al. concluded that analytical 
support tools were deemed desirable by industrial practitioners [18]. This is especially relevant for 
inexperienced designers of collaborative robot systems since previous experience is one of the main 
factors that can aid the risk assessment process from the perspective of the interview participants, as 
also found by other researchers [13], [18]. 
In current research the balance between advanced analytical capabilities and ease of use is still a 
challenge [18]. To this end, this thesis presents a systematic process for the hazard analysis of 
collaborative robot systems that can assist system designers in early stages of system development. 
To develop and validate this systematic process, this work analysed hazards and hazard sources that 
can typically be observed in collaborative robot systems and mitigation strategies that are typically 
used to reduce the risks related to these hazards. This is a main contribution of this work.  
The hazard sources hereby first revolve around the collaborative robot system, which consists of the 
robot itself, with extensive considerations for the robot speed, the programming/process that is 
followed by the robot, and finally the end effector/tool that is attached to the end of arm. Especially 
the robot speed is also defined by Vicentini as an important factor in assessing hazards in collaborative 
systems [32]. The frequency in which a human operator must access the collaborative workspace, 
which is a core consideration in Vicentini´s work [32], is something that is not considered by the 
industrial practitioners that have participated in the interviews for this work. Nonetheless, the 
importance of this factor should not be dismissed. Therefore, the access requirements are still 
considered in the suggested systematic approach.  
A hazard source recognised by both industrial practitioners as well as the literature base is the end of 
arm tool, together with the workpiece or object that is handled or processed by the system. This 
==PAGE:87==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Discussion 
Page 79 
 
source is also described in ISO/TS 15066 which is the applicable standardisation document regarding 
collaborative robot systems [19]. While the importance of this hazard source is not disputed, other 
sources should receive an equal amount of attention. For example, it has been discovered in this work 
that the environment in which the robot system is placed is an additional relevant source of hazards 
besides the collaborative robot system itself. It can be related to attached machinery or to 
environmental conditions. Also, access possibilities to the shared workspace must be considered when 
looking at the environment. These sources can also be observed in the risk factors for collaborative 
systems according to Berx [27].  
The human factors, which are also included in the risk factors [27], seem to receive limited 
consideration from industrial practitioners. While human error is considered by some, the majority of 
interview participants did not speak about specific considerations for this hazard source. Even though 
it is part of existing safety standards like ISO 12100 in the form of expected misuse of the machine 
[22] and it is described by research as a hazard source, for example in [10], [33], [34], [37]. The same 
can be observed for different phases of the life cycle of collaborative systems. While most accidents 
occur during maintenance operations [36], this thesis concluded that this phase of life likely receives 
less attention from practitioners in the identification of hazards.  
A consideration by industrial practitioners in the design of collaborative systems are different 
operational modes, i.e. an automatic mode and a manual mode. In automatic mode programmed 
tasks are executed, while manual mode allows for direct operator input [20]. These modes should 
receive more consideration in the identification of hazards. Mode awareness and mode error can lead 
to hazardous situations according to different researchers [10], [28], [33], [34]. This consideration, 
which comes from a socio-technical perspective on the safety analysis, is something that system 
designers should take notice of. These deviations between hazards that are considered in industrial 
practice and hazard sources that are deemed relevant by researchers are a contribution of this work.  
Besides hazard sources, this work also draws conclusions concerning hazard mitigation. The choice of 
suitable mitigation strategies for collaborative systems is hereby noted as a challenge by the interview 
participants. Research is already trying to address this issue [15], [47], however, a structured 
framework to orient mitigation strategies according to hazards that are present in a system is still 
missing [15]. As part of this thesis, the systematic process for the hazard analysis described some 
considerations that could be useful for such a framework, specifically which mitigation strategies are 
applicable to which collaborative operational mode and which operational mode is suited for a given 
application based on certain factors.  
The mitigation strategies that could be used to reduce risks according to research have been described 
in Chapter 2.2.1.2 of this work. A contribution of this thesis also lies in narrowing down these 
mitigation strategies. In industrial practice, hazards are reduced through reducing the interaction 
energy by removing sharp edges and by reducing the speed of the robot system. In addition, access 
==PAGE:88==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Discussion 
Page 80 
 
to hazard sources is removed for example through specific considerations for the layout or through 
encapsulating hazard sources. Technical measures such as a laser range scanner or limits to the robot 
movements are further applied. This, more limited list of mitigation strategies that are applied in 
practice, could help make future research more applicable for practitioners. 
Similar to hazard sources, which have been recognised by research but not by the majority of interview 
participants, hazard mitigation strategies that should receive more attention can also be described. 
Creating predictable robot behaviour, for example through different means of communication, is 
discussed by individual interviewees but also by research [10], [28], [33], [34].  
This concludes the main findings and results of this work. In the following, it should be evaluated if 
the goals set for this project have been achieved and what the results cannot reveal through examining 
the limitations of this work.  
6.2 Critical Reflection  
Chapter 3 of this thesis described and introduced objectives for this project and requirements for the 
solution that should be developed as part of this thesis. The main aim hereby, which also corresponds 
to the impetus, is to develop a systematic approach for the hazard analysis for collaborative robot 
systems. This process was initially developed based on the theoretical research in Chapter 5.1.2 and 
iterated based on the interview findings in Chapter 5.2.3. Therefore, the main objective was 
completed. This systematic approach according to the set objectives was supposed to focus on the 
hazard identification and hazard mitigation steps of the risk assessment process. This aim was 
achieved, first by focusing on different risk factors that must be evaluated to see if a collaborative 
operational mode can be used in a given application. Second, by providing different hazard mitigation 
strategies for each operational mode. The hazard identification and mitigation should hereby include 
a task-based perspective. This objective was achieved through the focus on a certain set of 
applications as the basis for the schematic. Furthermore, the process should consider a socio-technical 
perspective. While some socio-technical considerations, for example the inclusion of human error as 
a hazard source as well as predictable robot behaviour and communication between the actors as 
hazard mitigation strategies, are part of the process, the focus is not as clear. Therefore, this objective 
is only partially completed.  
A further goal for the systematic process is to balance advanced capabilities and ease of use. Ease of 
use for the system designer is provided through different exit points in the process. Furthermore, 
complexity is removed from the following process steps (i.e., the detailed iteration of the risk 
assessment). Therefore, this objective was achieved. The approach is further practically usable, which 
was an additional objective, through the limitation to one page. Presenting the information in a 
condensed way helps improve the usability. To this end, the project was described to also aim at 
testing and validating different assumptions through the views of industrial practitioners. This was 
achieved through the conducted expert interviews. Finally, the approach should not be fragmented. 
==PAGE:89==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Discussion 
Page 81 
 
This objective was not fully achieved since a detailed iteration of the risk assessment must still follow 
after the process has been followed by the system designer. Therefore, the approach does not provide 
complete guidance on the risk assessment for collaborative robot systems. Nonetheless, the main 
objectives set for this work were achieved.  
However, some limitations of this work and of the results that are presented in the discussion of this 
work must also be examined. As introduced in Chapter 4, initially a second iteration was planned. This 
iteration was supposed aim at analysing assumptions with a larger dataset through a survey with 
industry experts. However, time-related constraints made it infeasible to go ahead with this iteration. 
These constraints are mostly related to the expert interviews. While the original project plan aimed 
to complete the set of twelve interviews by the 20th of May, they could only be completed two weeks 
later even though the interview process was started earlier than initially planned. Since the time to 
set up and analyse a survey for reviewing the work did not include any time buffers in the original 
project plan, it was decided to skip this second step to rather focus on a thorough analysis of the 
interview data.  
A further limitation of the results of this study comes through the connection to Universal Robots (UR), 
which might reduce the generalisability of the results. First, the analysis of typical use-cases presented 
in Chapter 5.1.1 relies on marketing material published by UR. Second, all expert interview participants 
are in a business relationship with UR since they are all working for a system integrator of UR products. 
These participants were, however, the best available candidates for the interviews, which is a factor 
that must be considered in choosing interview participants. Their opinions might include certain biases 
to favour both Universal Robots but also collaborative robotics in general. This must be considered in 
the conclusions which are drawn based on the participants’ quotes. A further limitation of these 
conclusions is the limited dataset of only twelve interviews. While the amount is large enough to allow 
for some conclusions, they cannot be confirmed or denied with certainty since other experts might 
have differing opinions – especially in this area of research which is highly reliant on individual 
experiences and knowledge as also discovered as part of this work. In addition, it was not possible to 
cover all assumptions in all twelve interviews, which further limits the data available to draw 
conclusions.  
Nonetheless, the chosen approach for this work provided new insights into how collaborative robot 
systems are being used today. Furthermore, challenges faced by industrial practitioners have been 
discovered as well as strategies to work around these challenges. Most importantly, this work 
described a set of typical hazard sources as well as mitigation strategies to reduce the risks these 
sources present. This description of typical hazard and typical mitigation strategies does not claim 
completeness and it is subject to changes in light of further technical development, however, it 
provides a starting point for a discussion on a more systematic method for the hazard analysis for 
collaborative robot systems.  
==PAGE:90==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Discussion 
Page 82 
 
6.3 Future Work 
Based on the results and insights that can be taken from this work, considerations both for industrial 
practitioners and also for future research should be examined in the following. Industrial practitioners, 
referring to system designers of collaborative robot systems, should place more emphasis on certain 
hazard sourced and mitigation strategies that have been discovered through theoretical research. 
Namely, this includes identifying hazards during maintenance operations. In addition, human error as 
a hazard source should receive more pronounced attention. Similarly, communication between the 
actors of a collaborative system is a strategy to reduce risks that should be applied more regularly, for 
example to avoid mode error through informing the human operator about the current state of the 
robot system.  
Besides these specific considerations, practitioners could strive towards pronounced rules, guidelines, 
or checklists in the early stages of evaluating a possible collaborative robot system. This would limit 
the reliance on individual expert experiences, and it could help to set up the continued design process 
in a structured way. For this purpose, the systematic process described as part of this work can be a 
starting point. Applying, and hereby improving, this process, could help the design of collaborative 
robot systems in the future.  
The assumptions and findings of this work could be the subject of further study in the field of risk 
assessment. The typical hazards and mitigation strategies have been described based on a limited 
dataset and broader considerations, both from industry experts but also from other researchers, could 
help to improve these descriptions.  
To better understand the implications of the results of this work, future studies could address a more 
systematic approach for the detailed iteration of the risk assessment that must follow in later design 
stages. The process could hereby, for example, be guided by going through the collaborative system 
“part by part” or by following the process/movement of the robot arm. Generally, both for continuing 
to work towards a more systematic risk assessment but also more broadly in the research about 
machine safety, the focus should be on industrial users' needs. That way more people will be able to 
apply and use the results of research and hereby researchers can in the best way assist in creating safe 
and comfortable working environments. Furthermore, as discovered in this work, research should 
strive towards considering a socio-technical perspective in the assessment of safety-critical aspects in 
collaborative robot systems.  
A possibility for future work is also to strive towards automating the risk assessment process. The 
description of typical hazards, mitigation strategies used to reduce the risks stemming from these 
sources, as well as other considerations depending on the use-case type are a starting point for this 
purpose. More data must be gathered and validated to automate the risk assessment. Furthermore, 
different risk mitigation strategies still need to be oriented towards the hazard sources. Some 
considerations will always be machine specific, for example considerations regarding the environment 
==PAGE:91==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Conclusion 
Page 83 
 
in which the system is placed and regarding general design conditions and requirements. However, a 
large share of the work of a risk assessment could, based on the results of this work, potentially be 
automated with a large database of typical hazards and mitigation strategies to ensure validity of the 
results. An open question, should complete automation or at least partial automation of the risk 
assessment process be achieved, is the liability for accidents. The CE-marking and the declaration of 
conformity, which are the results of a conscientious risk assessment process in which the system 
designer spends effort in following a systematic process to discover all relevant risks and hazards that 
must be mitigated through some manner, are currently used to define liability [12]. The designer who 
signed the declaration of conformity is liable for any accidents that occur with the machine. In case of 
an accident, they have to prove that they have tried conscientiously to identify and remove/reduce all 
foreseeable and relevant risks. Should the risk assessment be completed (partially) automated would 
the question of liability need to be newly defined. Automating the risk assessment with a valid 
database covering all possible scenarios of designing a collaborative robot system would, however, 
require significant effort and resources.  
7 Conclusion 
Collaborative robot systems are safety-critical due to the close physical collaboration of human 
operators and robot systems  [5], [10], [14]–[16]. Strict safety requirements are hereby a limitation in 
the broader adaptation of these systems [14]. Especially the risk assessment is seen as challenging. 
This thesis aims to provide guidance and support for conducting the hazard analysis of collaborative 
robot systems, focused on inexperienced users. More support is needed since new hazard sources 
must be considered in the analysis [15], [27], [36], [37], [39] and since new mitigation strategies exist 
to reduce the risks related to these sources [15], [22], [28], [34], [35], [46]. In addition, a more 
systematic method for the hazard analysis is needed because tools and methods currently applied 
typically rely on previous experience of the person conducting the risk assessment [13], [18]. 
Therefore, the thesis should help to lower entry barriers for first-time automation users, and it should 
support the creation of safe and comfortable working environments for human operators working 
with collaborative systems.  
To achieve this, first the current landscape of safety standards relevant to the risk assessment of 
collaborative robot systems, like ISO 12100:2011 [22] or ISO 10218:2012 [23], was assessed as part of 
the thesis. Within this, differences between the current applicable ISO 10218:2012 and the new draft 
ISO 10218:2020, especially regarding changed definitions about collaborative robot systems [20], have 
been noted. This was followed by a structured literature review in which technical literature from the 
research field was introduced. An analysis of typical use-cases through the assessment of case study 
videos laid the basis for a systematic process for the hazard analysis of collaborative systems. 
Afterwards, a series of expert interviews build the foundation for improvements of this process 
derived from industrial practitioners' views and opinions.  
==PAGE:92==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Conclusion 
Page 84 
 
Based on the conducted research it can be confirmed that previous experience is a core factor in 
completing a risk assessment. This work managed to capture some of the experiences of industry 
experts through interviews. Typical hazards and typical mitigation strategies have been summarised 
with some validity based on the statements of participants. Typical hazards revolve around the robot 
system, which consists of the robot arm itself where the speed of the arm is an important factor and 
the tool which is attached to the end of the arm. In addition, the environment can present hazards, 
either related to attached machinery or related to environmental conditions. Also, access possibilities 
to the shared workspace must be considered when looking at the environment.  
Most commonly, hazards are reduced by lowering the interaction forces of the robot system. This can 
be achieved with a lower speed. It can also be achieved by removing sharp edges or corners. Such 
edges or corners present hazard sources that could also be encapsulated to reduce risks. Other 
possibilities to remove access to hazard sources are also applied in industrial practice. Finally, the 
robot system could be limited through technical measures either in its movements through joint limits 
and safety planes or in its speed through laser range scanners.  
These conclusions are mostly in line with previous findings from the conducted literature review. 
However, some aspects that receive limited attention in industrial practice but that were found during 
the theoretical research should be introduced since they could improve safety aspects of collaborative 
robot systems in the future. Namely, accidents could be prevented by placing more emphasis on 
identifying hazards during maintenance operations, since these tasks are more prone to result in 
accidents [36]. In addition, improved communication between the different actors of the socio-
technical collaborative robot system could reduce risks related to certain hazard sources like missing 
mode awareness [10], [28], [33], [34]. In general, improvements to the risk assessment process could 
benefit from a more socio-technical perspective. Specifically, this could include considerations for 
human error or expected misuse of a machine as a hazard source [20], [22].  
With these findings, this work provides information that can help guide system designers of 
collaborative robot systems in the early design stages of collaborative systems. The systematic process 
presented in Figure 8 is one of the main contributions of this thesis. Hereby, the aim of lowering entry 
barriers for first-time automation customers was achieved. This thesis further concludes with 
recommendations for further study. Since only information and guidance for the early design stages, 
which are especially reliant on experts´ experiences, is provided in this work, the later design stages 
remain open for further study. The correct application of collaborative operational modes like Power 
and Force Limiting (PFL) and the validation of interaction forces is a challenge noted by industry 
experts. Working towards a more systematic process in the later design stages, for example also in 
the validation of interaction forces, that balances advanced capabilities and ease of use [18], could 
further improve safety aspects of collaborative robot systems.

Appendix 6 
Interview Guide  
Section 1/Introduction, 5 Minutes, background and personal experience 
Purpose: 
• 
I am looking to learn more about the risk assessment for collaborative robots, but why? 
• 
Johannes Thiem, MSc DTU, Industrial Engineering 
• 
Personal Experience with difficulty conducting RA for cobot systems 
• 
During master reach out to UR for internship 
• 
As part of that figure out a topic for Thesis, i.e. one specific issue with the risk assessment 
process for collaborative robots  
• 
Analysis of hazards both in identification but also in mitigation → limited data in theoretical 
research, want to gather data that is related to practical experience (Lacking for myself but 
also in research in general)  
• 
With this I would now start the recording so I can focus on our conversation today instead of 
taking notes 
 
First off, some notes on your background: What is your current role? How does that relate to Risk 
Assessments?  
Section 2, 10 Minutes, previous risk assessments and current process 
Can you describe the process you follow when conducting a risk assessment?  
Which tools have you used last time?  
Was there anything you particularly struggled with? (A1) 
If yes, could your experience have been improved by the addition of something? (A1) 
Did you struggle with finding hazards/hazard sources? Could you have benefitted from more support 
in the identification? 
Which hazards did you identify in the risk assessment? Which hazard sources did you consider? (A3)  
Which machine life phases did you consider when identifying hazards? 
How were you able to decide on suitable mitigation strategies? 
Section 3, 10 Minutes, Cobots and foundation for improvements 
What application have you worked on last/What was the cobot task in your last project? Can you share 
some pictures?  (Tools, EOA, ...) 
==PAGE:108==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Conclusion 
Page 100 
 
Generally speaking, what type of application are you working with? Which application type has the 
biggest share of your workload? Which application type is most demanded from your customers? 
From the top of your head: what are hazards you have seen in different (collaborative) systems? (A2) 
Which hazard categories do you observe? 
What part of the system receives most of your consideration?  
What are some mitigation strategies you have used? (A2) How did you mitigate the hazards in the last 
system you worked with? Link this to the specific hazards that have been mentioned by the participant 
Which safety functions or components did you include?  
Is there anything that helped mitigate hazards that was not a safety feature? (Change Layout, change 
physical parameters, use something to remove access like a shelve)  
Backup: What is your opinion on using fences for collaborative robots?   
Section 4/Closing, 5 Minutes, Final thoughts 
Did you certify a truly collaborative system yet? (underlying) Do you see the need for such systems? If 
there is time 
We have finished the questions I wanted to ask. Is there anything you would like to add? Anything you 
could not mention yet in the interview?

Appendix 7 
E Mail used in the Campaign to inquire about Interviews 
Subject: Help improve the Risk Assessment Process for Collaborative Robots 
 
Dear NAME,  
I am contacting you to inquire whether you would be open to joining an expert interview for my 
current master thesis studies.  
 
My name is Johannes Thiem, and in my current role, I am a Master Thesis Student for Universal Robots. 
The thesis aims to work towards improving the risk assessment process. For this, I want to gather data 
through expert interviews. First, I want to discuss currently used risk assessment methods. And 
second, I want to learn about experiences gathered through building different collaborative robot 
systems and performing risk assessments for these systems.  
 
For this purpose, I am contacting you as one of Universal Robots System Integrator Partners. I believe 
that your practical experience can add valuable information to my thesis. I would like you to help 
improve the risk assessment process for cobots through your experience and knowledge.  
If you would be interested in sharing your opinions and experiences, I would be delighted to schedule 
a short interview. Depending on your preference, the interview could both be online or physical. If 
you are interested, please respond to this E-Mail or call me (+491590 1069260). I would also 
appreciate if you forward this Mail to other colleagues of yours that work in this field.  
 
Thank you very much for your feedback and for getting back to me.  
Kind Regards,   
Johannes Thiem

Appendix 8 
Template: Consent to the Processing of Personal Data 
Consent to the processing of personal data 
 
Data controller student: Frithjof Johannes Thiem 
 
Title of Master’s thesis: Systematic Hazard Analysis for Collaborative Robot Systems 
 
Supervisor of the Master’s thesis: Xinxin Zhang, Michael Bruhn Barfod, Jakob Ormhøj 
 
Description of the project, including the purpose of the data processing and which personal data is 
processed: 
 
This project aims at systematically analysing two steps of the risk assessment process for collaborative 
robot systems. More specifically, the Hazards Identification and the Hazard Mitigation should be 
analysed further. Both steps are vital in reaching a safe system through the risk assessment and CE-
marking process. Nonetheless, both steps are currently ill-defined in theoretical research. In current 
industrial practice, these steps heavily rely on the expertise and experience of the person conducting 
the risk assessment, as they are heavily dependent on the system itself, as well as several other factors 
like the type of application or the environment in which the system is placed. Through this project, a 
broad understanding of typical hazards in collaborative robot systems should be established. 
 
For this purpose, interviews are conducted which aim at gathering information related to the 
aforementioned project scope. Specifically, the interviews should support the process of establishing 
a broad understanding of typical hazards. Personal data in this context may be collected in the form 
of personal identification details such as qualifications, date of employment, position, area of work. 
In the processing of personal data this data will be anonymized through a key that separates the 
collected data from the individual. The personal data as well as the key will be deleted when Master’s 
thesis has been assessed and the deadline for complaints has expired. 
 
 
==PAGE:111==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Conclusion 
Page 103 
 
I hereby consent to the aforementioned student’s processing of data concerning me in connection 
with his/her degree programme at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). My personal data will 
be used in the aforementioned assignment/project/Master’s thesis. I give my consent for: 
• 
The processing of my data in the assignment/project/Master’s thesis. 
• 
My data may be disclosed to DTU and to any external co-examiner in connection with 
supervision and assessment. 
• 
My data may be published in anonymous form in connection with the publication of the 
project or Master’s thesis. This clause may be removed completely if it is not assessed to be 
necessary. The assessment must be made for each individual project. 
 
 
Date:   
 
 
Name:   
 
 
Signature:   
 
The consent may be withdrawn at any time with future effect. Consent can be withdrawn via this 
email address: fjth@universal-robots.com  
 
 
 
Information to the data subject 
In accordance with the rules in the General Data Protection Regulation, as data controller the student 
must inform the data subjects of their rights in conjunction with the processing of the data. The 
student registers and processes personal data under the authority of Article 6(1) a) of the General 
Data Protection Regulation. Sensitive data such as health data or data concerning racial or ethnic 
origin, political, religious or philosophical beliefs or trade-union membership is registered and 
processed under the authority of Article 9(2) a) of the General Data Protection Regulation. Both 
articles of the Regulation give access to process data with the explicit content of the data subject. 
 
Processing and storage 
The student will treat the personal data as confidential. The data will be stored until the 
assignment/project/Master’s thesis has been assessed and the deadline for complaints concerning 
the assessment has expired. 
 
==PAGE:112==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Conclusion 
Page 104 
 
Disclosure of data 
The data will not be disclosed to any other party unless consent has been given. 
 
Data access 
Data subjects may contact the student at any time in order to obtain a copy of the data. 
 
Rectification of data 
If the data subject believes that incorrect data has been registered, the student can be asked to rectify 
the information. This means that the student must rectify the data or make a note that the data is 
incorrect and register the correct data. The data subject may require the student to disregard the data 
until it has been determined which data is correct. 
 
Revocation of consent and erasure of data 
If the student has obtained the consent of the data subject to process the data, the data subject may 
revoke this consent at any time. The student may therefore not continue to process the data after the 
consent has been withdrawn. 
The data subject has the right to the erasure of data which the student has registered concerning the 
data subject in question, if the data is no longer necessary for the purpose for which it was collected. 
The data must also be erased if the data subject withdraws consent to the processing, or if the data 
has been processed unlawfully by mistake. The data subject may not require the erasure of data that 
has been archived in accordance with the Danish Archives Act in Aarhus University’s archive system. 
 
Complaints to the Danish Data Protection Agency 
Data subjects may lodge complaints about the processing of the data to the Danish Data Protection 
Agency at: dt@datatilsynet.dk.
